However, the Israelites did not expel the Geshurites or the Maachathites, allowing them to dwell among the Israelites to this day. The tribe of Levi received no inheritance; their inheritance was the sacrifices made by fire to the LORD God of Israel. The tribe of Reuben received an inheritance according to their families, with their territory stretching from Aroer by the river Arnon, including the city in the midst of the river, and extending across the plain by Medeba. Their lands included Heshbon and its surrounding cities, Dibon, Bamothbaal, Bethbaalmeon, Jahaza, Kedemoth, Mephaath, Kirjathaim, Sibmah, Zarethshahar in the mount of the valley, Bethpeor, Ashdothpisgah, Bethjeshimoth, and all the cities of the plain and the kingdom of Sihon king of the Amorites. Balaam, the son of Beor the soothsayer, was slain by the Israelites among those defeated. The border of the children of Reuben was Jordan. This was their inheritance, including the cities and villages.

Moses also gave inheritance to the tribe of Gad according to their families. Their territory included Jazer, all the cities of Gilead, and half the land of the children of Ammon up to Aroer before Rabbah; from Heshbon to Ramathmizpeh and Betonim; and from Mahanaim to the border of Debir. In the valley were Betharam, Bethnimrah, Succoth, and Zaphon, the remainder of the kingdom of Sihon king of Heshbon, with Jordan as its border, extending to the edge of the sea of Chinnereth on the east side of Jordan. This was the inheritance of the children of Gad, including their cities and villages.

The half tribe of Manasseh also received their inheritance from Moses, which included all Bashan, the kingdom of Og king of Bashan, and all the towns of Jair in Bashan, totaling sixty cities. Half of Gilead, and the cities of Ashtaroth and Edrei in Bashan, were given to the descendants of Machir the son of Manasseh. These lands were distributed by Moses for inheritance in the plains of Moab, on the east side of Jordan by Jericho. The tribe of Levi, however, received no inheritance as the LORD God of Israel was their inheritance.

The lands inherited by the children of Israel in Canaan were distributed by Eleazar the priest, Joshua the son of Nun, and the heads of the fathers of the tribes of Israel. Their inheritance was determined by lot, as commanded by the LORD through Moses, for the nine tribes and the half tribe. Moses had previously given the inheritance of two and a half tribes on the other side of Jordan, but the Levites received no inheritance among them. The children of Joseph, being two tribes, Manasseh and Ephraim, received no part of the Levites' land, except for cities to dwell in, with their suburbs for their cattle and substance. As commanded by the LORD, the children of Israel divided the land.

Caleb the son of Jephunneh approached Joshua in Gilgal, reminding him of the LORD's promise made in Kadeshbarnea concerning the land Caleb had spied out. Caleb, who was forty years old at the time of the spying and had followed the LORD fully, was promised the land he trod on as an inheritance for himself and his descendants. Now, at eighty-five years old, Caleb declared himself as strong as he was then and requested the land promised to him by Moses.Caleb, at the age of 85, requested the mountain region he had spied out, as promised by the LORD, despite the presence of the Anakims and their fortified cities. Joshua blessed Caleb and gave him Hebron as an inheritance. This act was in recognition of Caleb's unwavering faith in the LORD God of Israel. Hebron, previously known as Kirjatharba, became Caleb's inheritance because he wholly followed the LORD. The land then had rest from war.

The allocation of land continued with the tribe of Judah receiving their portion, extending to the border of Edom in the south, encompassing the wilderness of Zin. Their southern border stretched from the shore of the Salt Sea, moving through various landmarks and ending at the river of Egypt. The eastern border was along the Salt Sea to the end of Jordan, while the northern border extended from the bay of the sea at the Jordan's end, through several locations, to the great sea. This defined the territory of Judah according to their families.

Caleb was also given a portion among the children of Judah, as per the LORD's command to Joshua, including the city of Arba, father of Anak, which is Hebron. Caleb drove out the three sons of Anak from there. He then offered his daughter Achsah as a wife to whoever could capture Kirjathsepher. Othniel, Caleb's nephew, succeeded and married Achsah. Upon her arrival, Achsah persuaded Othniel to request a field from Caleb, which led to her receiving both upper and nether springs as a blessing.

The cities and territories belonging to the tribe of Judah were extensively listed, covering from Kabzeel in the south near Edom to various other locations, totaling twenty-nine cities with their villages. Additional cities in the valley and surrounding areas were also mentioned, highlighting the extensive inheritance of the tribe of Judah according to their families.If a man finds a virgin who is not betrothed, and he seizes her and lies with her, and they are discovered, then the man who lay with her shall give fifty shekels of silver to the girl's father, and she shall become his wife because he violated her; he cannot divorce her all his days. A man shall not marry his father's wife, nor uncover his father's bed. Anyone who is emasculated or has his male organ cut off shall not enter the assembly of the LORD. A person born out of wedlock shall not enter the assembly of the LORD up to the tenth generation. An Ammonite or Moabite shall not enter the assembly of the LORD, even to the tenth generation, because they did not meet you with bread and water on your way out of Egypt, and because they hired Balaam to curse you. However, the LORD your God refused to listen to Balaam and turned the curse into a blessing for you, because the LORD your God loves you. You shall not seek their peace or their prosperity all your days. You shall not abhor an Edomite, for he is your brother; you shall not abhor an Egyptian, because you were a stranger in his land. The children born to them may enter the assembly of the LORD in the third generation. When you are in camp against your enemies, keep away from everything impure. If there is among you anyone who is not clean because of a nocturnal emission, then he must go outside the camp; he may not reenter the camp. But when evening comes, he shall bathe himself in water, and at sunset, he may reenter the camp. You shall have a designated area outside the camp to relieve yourself. With your equipment, you shall have a trowel; when you relieve yourself outside, you shall dig a hole with it and then cover up your excrement. For the LORD your God moves about in your camp to protect you and to deliver your enemies to you. Your camp must be holy, so that he will not see among you anything indecent and turn away from you. You shall not hand over to his master a slave who has escaped from his master to you. He shall live with you, in your midst, in the place he chooses within one of your towns, wherever it suits him; you shall not oppress him. There shall be no cult prostitute of the daughters of Israel, nor a cult prostitute of the sons of Israel. You shall not bring the fee of a prostitute or the wages of a dog into the house of the LORD your God for any vow, for both of these are an abomination to the LORD your God. You shall not charge interest on loans to your brother, interest on money, interest on food, interest on anything that is lent for interest. To a foreigner you may charge interest, but to your brother you shall not charge interest, that the LORD your God may bless you in all that you undertake in the land that you are entering to take possession of it. When you vow a vow to the LORD your God, you shall not delay fulfilling it, for the LORD your God will surely require it of you, and it would be sin in you. But if you refrain from vowing, it will not be sin in you. You shall be careful to do what has passed your lips, for you have voluntarily vowed to the LORD your God what you have promised with your mouth."Perform a freewill offering as you have vowed to the LORD your God. When entering your neighbor's vineyard, you may eat grapes to your satisfaction but must not take any with you. In your neighbor's standing grain, you may pluck ears by hand but not use a sickle. If a man divorces his wife due to finding 'uncleanness' in her, she may remarry. However, if her second husband divorces or dies, she cannot return to her first husband, as this is abominable before the LORD. Newlyweds should not be disturbed for one year to ensure happiness. Taking essential items as pledge, like millstones, is forbidden. Stealing and selling a fellow Israelite is punishable by death. Follow the priests' instructions on leprosy carefully. Remember the LORD's actions towards Miriam after leaving Egypt. Do not enter a borrower's house for a pledge; let them bring it out. Return a poor man's pledge before sunset for his comfort. Pay a day laborer on time, especially if poor, to avoid sin. Children should not be punished for their parents' sins, and vice versa. Do not pervert justice for strangers, orphans, or take a widow's garment as pledge. Leave forgotten sheaves in the field for the needy, as a reminder of your bondage in Egypt. Do not overharvest your olive trees or vineyard; leave some for the needy. In disputes, judges should acquit the innocent and condemn the guilty. Punishments should be fair, not exceeding forty lashes to avoid degrading your brother. Do not muzzle an ox while it treads grain. If a man dies childless, his brother should marry the widow to preserve the family line. The firstborn of such a union carries on the deceased brother's name."If a man does not wish to marry his brother's widow, she shall go to the elders at the gate and declare his refusal to uphold his brother's name in Israel. The elders will then summon him, and if he persists in his refusal, the widow shall remove his shoe, spit in his face, and declare, "This is what is done to the man who does not build up his brother's house," and his name shall be known as 'The house of him that had his shoe loosed.' In cases of conflict, if a wife intervenes by seizing the assailant by his genitals, her hand shall be cut off without pity. Diverse weights and measures are forbidden; only just and perfect measures are allowed to ensure a long life in the land given by the LORD. Remember the actions of Amalek against Israel and ensure their memory is blotted out upon settling in the land given by the LORD. Upon entering the land and harvesting its first fruits, they shall be taken to the place the LORD chooses and offered to the priest, declaring the history of Israel's suffering in Egypt and deliverance by the LORD. The first fruits are then set before the LORD in worship. Every third year, the tithes are to be given to the Levites, strangers, fatherless, and widows to ensure they are fed, and a declaration made that the commandments have been followed without transgression. Prayers are made for the LORD to look down from heaven and bless Israel and the land given to them, a land flowing with milk and honey. The children of Joseph questioned Joshua about their inheritance, feeling entitled to more due to their size and the blessings they had received. Joshua encouraged them to clear the forests in the land of the Perizzites and the giants if the land of Ephraim was too narrow. Despite their complaints about the hill not being sufficient and the Canaanites in the valley having iron chariots, Joshua assured them of their strength and the promise of more land.

The congregation of the children of Israel gathered at Shiloh, where the tabernacle was set up, and the land was subdued before them. Seven tribes had not yet received their inheritance, prompting Joshua to organize a survey of the land to divide it accordingly. The Levites received no portion as the priesthood of the LORD was their inheritance. The tribes of Gad, Reuben, and half the tribe of Manasseh had already received their inheritance east of Jordan, as given by Moses.

Upon completing the land division, Joshua received Timnathserah in Mount Ephraim as his inheritance, as commanded by the Lord. The inheritances were distributed by Eleazar the priest, Joshua, and the tribal leaders in Shiloh before the Lord.

The Lord instructed Joshua to establish cities of refuge for those who accidentally killed someone, allowing them to seek asylum and avoid vengeance until a fair trial. The cities designated as refuges were Kedesh in Galilee, Shechem in Mount Ephraim, Hebron in the mountain of Judah, Bezer in the wilderness, Ramoth in Gilead, and Golan in Bashan.

They stated, "our burnt offerings, and with our sacrifices, and with our peace offerings; that your children may not say to our children in time to come, Ye have no part in the LORD. Therefore, we said that it shall be, when they should so say to us or to our generations in time to come, that we may say again, Behold the pattern of the altar of the LORD, which our fathers made, not for burnt offerings, nor for sacrifices; but it is a witness between us and you. God forbid that we should rebel against the LORD, and turn this day from following the LORD, to build an altar for burnt offerings, for meat offerings, or for sacrifices, beside the altar of the LORD our God that is before his tabernacle." Upon hearing this, Phinehas and the leaders were pleased, recognizing that the LORD was among them because they had not committed trespass against the LORD. The children of Israel blessed God, deciding not to battle against the Reubenites and Gadites. The altar was named Ed, signifying it as a witness that the LORD is God.

Afterward, Joshua, now old and stricken in age, called for all Israel and its leaders, reminding them of the LORD's deeds and the lands divided among the tribes as inheritance. He urged them to be courageous, to keep and do all written in the law of Moses, not to associate with the remaining nations nor adopt their practices, but to cleave unto the LORD. Joshua warned against turning back to these nations, as it would lead to the LORD no longer driving out these nations before them, becoming snares and traps. He emphasized that all the good things promised by the LORD had come to pass and warned that disobedience to the LORD's covenant would lead to destruction. Joshua gathered all the tribes at Shechem, calling for the elders and leaders, signaling another significant moment of covenant renewal and instruction.Joshua gathered the people, including their judges and officers, and recounted the history of their ancestors, emphasizing their transition from idolatry to serving the one true God. He reminded them of Abraham's journey, the deliverance from Egypt, and the conquests in Canaan, attributing their successes to God's intervention rather than their own might. Joshua urged the Israelites to serve the Lord with sincerity and truth, rejecting the idols their ancestors worshipped. He presented a choice between serving the gods of their ancestors or the Lord, declaring his own household's commitment to the Lord. The people reaffirmed their dedication to serving the Lord, acknowledging His role in their history and deliverance. Joshua warned them about the consequences of forsaking the Lord for other gods. The people insisted on serving the Lord, leading Joshua to establish a covenant at Shechem, setting a stone as a witness to their commitment. Joshua's leadership concluded with his death at 110 years old, and he was buried in his inheritance in Timnathserah. He reminded them of Abraham's journey, the deliverance from Egypt, and their conquests in Canaan, attributing their successes to God's intervention. Joshua urged the Israelites to serve the Lord sincerely and truthfully, rejecting their ancestors' idols. He presented a choice between serving their ancestors' gods or the Lord, declaring his household's commitment to the Lord. The people reaffirmed their dedication to serving the Lord, recognizing His role in their history and deliverance. Joshua warned of the consequences of forsaking the Lord for other gods. The people insisted on serving the Lord, leading Joshua to establish a covenant at Shechem, setting a stone as a witness to their commitment. Joshua's leadership ended with his death at 110, and he was buried in Timnathserah. His leadership and the covenant at Shechem had a lasting impact, with Israel continuing to serve the Lord during his lifetime and beyond.

The bones of Joseph, brought from Egypt, were buried in Shechem, in a parcel Jacob bought, becoming the inheritance of Joseph's children. Eleazar, Aaron's son, died and was buried in Ephraim. After Joshua's death, the Israelites inquired of the Lord about who should fight the Canaanites. The Lord designated Judah, leading to several victories and conquests, including Jerusalem. Caleb's daughter Achsah was given in marriage for capturing Kirjathsepher, leading to further territorial gains. Despite successes, some Canaanites remained in the land, living among the Israelites or as tributaries, illustrating the incomplete conquest of Canaan.The inhabitants of Accho, Zidon, Ahlab, Achzib, Helbah, Aphik, and Rehob were not driven out by the Israelites. The Asherites lived among the Canaanites, unable to expel them. Similarly, Naphtali did not remove the inhabitants of Bethshemesh and Bethanath, instead living among the Canaanites and making them tributaries. The Amorites forced the Danites into the mountains, refusing them access to the valley, yet the tribe of Joseph overcame the Amorites, making them tributaries. The territory of the Amorites extended from Akrabbim to the rock and upward.

An angel of the LORD appeared from Gilgal to Bochim, reminding the Israelites of their deliverance from Egypt and the covenant made with their ancestors. The angel rebuked them for not obeying God's command to destroy the altars of the land's inhabitants, warning that these nations would be thorns in their sides and their gods a snare. The Israelites wept at Bochim and offered sacrifices to the LORD. After Joshua dismissed them, each tribe went to possess their inheritance, serving the LORD throughout Joshua's lifetime and the elders who outlived him. Joshua died at 110 and was buried in Timnathheres, in Ephraim.

A new generation arose that did not know the LORD or His works for Israel, turning to serve Baalim and forsaking the LORD. They provoked the LORD's anger by following other gods, leading to their oppression by enemies. Despite this, the LORD raised judges to deliver them, yet they continued to disobey and follow other gods. The cycle of deliverance and disobedience continued, with the LORD's anger against Israel for breaking the covenant. The LORD decided not to drive out the remaining nations, using them to test Israel's faithfulness. These nations were left to teach war to the generations of Israelites who had not experienced the wars of Canaan.The Sidonians, and the Hivites in mount Lebanon, from mount Baalhermon to Hamath, were to test Israel's obedience to the LORD's commandments given through Moses. The Israelites lived among various Canaanite peoples, intermarrying and adopting their gods. This disobedience led to the LORD's anger, resulting in their subjugation to Chushanrishathaim of Mesopotamia for eight years. After crying out to the LORD, Othniel, Caleb's younger brother, was empowered by the LORD to deliver Israel, bringing peace for forty years until his death.

Israel's cycle of disobedience continued, leading to their oppression by Eglon, king of Moab, for eighteen years. Ehud, a left-handed Benjamite, was raised by the LORD as a deliverer. He crafted a double-edged dagger, hid it on his right thigh, and used it to kill Eglon after tricking him into a private meeting, claiming to have a secret message from God. Ehud then locked the doors, escaped, and rallied the Israelites to defeat the Moabites, securing peace for eighty years. Following Ehud, Shamgar defeated the Philistines, further delivering Israel.

When Ehud died, the Israelites again turned to evil, leading to their oppression by Jabin, king of Canaan, and his commander Sisera, who harshly ruled over them for twenty years. Deborah, a prophetess and judge, led Israel at this time. This act was meant to demonstrate the LORD's mighty power and instill reverence for Him among the people and throughout the earth. Upon hearing of the drying up of the Jordan, the hearts of the Amorite and Canaanite kings melted in fear, leaving them spiritless because of the Israelites.

The LORD then instructed Joshua to circumcise the Israelite males born in the wilderness, as those who had left Egypt had not been circumcised. This was to fulfill the covenant and remove the reproach of Egypt from them, marking a new beginning at Gilgal, where they also celebrated the Passover and began to eat the produce of Canaan, signifying the end of manna.

Joshua encountered a man with a drawn sword, who identified himself as the captain of the LORD's host, instructing Joshua to remove his shoes on the holy ground. This encounter reaffirmed Joshua's leadership and the divine support for Israel.

When the people shouted and the priests blew the trumpets, the walls of Jericho fell down flat, allowing the Israelites to take the city and destroy everything in it, except for Rahab and her family. The city was burned, but the valuable metals were put into the treasury of the LORD's house. Rahab and her family were spared and lived among the Israelites. Joshua pronounced a curse on anyone who would rebuild Jericho.

Joshua was to organize the people by tribes, families, and individuals to identify the transgressor. The guilty party, found to be Achan of the tribe of Judah, confessed to stealing a Babylonian garment, silver, and gold from the spoils of Jericho, hiding them in his tent. These items were retrieved and presented before Joshua and the Israelites.

Achan, along with his possessions, was taken to the Valley of Achor, where he and his family were stoned and burned, a punishment for his sin, which had brought trouble upon Israel. This act appeased the LORD's anger, and the place was henceforth known as the Valley of Achor.

And when they enquired and asked, they said, Gideon the son of Joash hath done this thing. 6:30 Then the men of the city said unto Joash, Bring out thy son, that he may die: because he hath cast down the altar of Baal, and because he hath cut down the grove that was by it. 6:31 And Joash said unto all that stood against him, Will ye plead for Baal? will ye save him? he that will plead for him, let him be put to death whilst it is yet morning: if he be a god, let him plead for himself, because one hath cast down his altar. 6:32 Therefore on that day he called him Jerubbaal, saying, Let Baal plead against him, because he hath thrown down his altar. 6:33 Then all the Midianites and the Amalekites and the children of the east were gathered together, and went over, and pitched in the valley of Jezreel. 6:34 But the Spirit of the LORD came upon Gideon, and he blew a trumpet; and if the dew be on the fleece only, and it be dry upon all the earth beside, then shall I know that thou wilt save Israel by mine hand, as thou hast said. 6:38 And it was so: for he rose up early on the morrow, and thrust the fleece together, and wringed the dew out of the fleece, a bowl full of water. 6:39 And Gideon said unto God, Let not thine anger be hot against me, and I will speak but this once: let me prove, I pray thee, but this once with the fleece; let it now be dry only upon the fleece, and upon all the ground let there be dew. 6:40 And God did so that night: for it was dry upon the fleece only, and there was dew on all the ground. 7:1 Then Jerubbaal, who is Gideon, and all the people that were with him, rose up early, and pitched beside the well of Harod: so that the host of the Midianites were on the north side of them, by the hill of Moreh, in the valley. 7:2 And the LORD said unto Gideon, The people that are with thee are too many for me to give the Midianites into their hands, lest Israel vaunt themselves against me, saying, Mine own hand hath saved me. 7:3 Now therefore go to, proclaim in the ears of the people, saying, Whosoever is fearful and afraid, let him return and depart early from mount Gilead. They approached the city, setting up camp on the north side with a valley between them and Ai. Joshua deployed about five thousand men in ambush between Bethel and Ai, on the city's west side. That night, Joshua moved into the valley.

When the king of Ai noticed the Israelites, he and his men went out for battle, unaware of the ambush behind the city. Joshua and all Israel pretended to be beaten and fled towards the wilderness, drawing the men of Ai away from the city. With no one left in Ai or Bethel, the city was left open.

The LORD instructed Joshua to stretch out his spear towards Ai, signaling the ambush to attack. They quickly took the city and set it on fire. The men of Ai, seeing the smoke, were unable to flee. The Israelites then turned back, defeating the men of Ai. They captured the king of Ai and brought him to Joshua.

After slaying all inhabitants of Ai in the field and the city, the Israelites took the cattle and spoil as prey, following the LORD's command. Joshua burned Ai, making it a heap of ruins. The king of Ai was hanged until evening, and his body was thrown at the city gate with a heap of stones over it.

Joshua then built an altar to the LORD on Mount Ebal, as Moses had commanded, using uncut stones. They offered burnt and peace offerings to the LORD. Joshua also wrote a copy of the law of Moses on the stones. All Israel, including elders, officers, judges, and strangers, stood before the ark of the covenant, with half facing Mount Gerizim and half facing Mount Ebal, to bless the people as commanded by Moses. Joshua read all the words of the law, blessings, and curses to the congregation, including women, children, and strangers. This event marked the fulfillment of Moses' commands and the continuation of Israel's covenant with the LORD.Joshua rose early, numbered the people, and led them with the elders of Israel to Ai. They camped on the north side, with a valley between them and Ai. Joshua set an ambush of about five thousand men between Bethel and Ai. That night, he moved into the valley.

The king of Ai and his men, unaware of the ambush, went out for battle. Joshua and all Israel pretended to flee towards the wilderness, drawing Ai's men away. With Ai and Bethel empty, the LORD instructed Joshua to signal the ambush. They took the city, set it on fire, and defeated Ai's men. The king of Ai was captured and brought to Joshua.

After slaying Ai's inhabitants and taking the spoil as commanded by the LORD, Joshua burned Ai to ruins. The king of Ai was hanged, and his body was thrown at the city gate with stones over it.

Joshua built an altar to the LORD on Mount Ebal with uncut stones, offering burnt and peace offerings. He wrote a copy of Moses' law on the stones. All Israel, including elders, officers, judges, and strangers, stood before the ark, half facing Mount Gerizim and half Mount Ebal, as Moses commanded. Joshua read the law, blessings, and curses to the congregation, fulfilling Moses' commands and continuing Israel's covenant with the LORD.

When the kings of the region heard of Joshua's victories, they gathered to fight against Israel. The Gibeonites, fearing Joshua, deceived him into making a peace treaty by pretending to come from a far country. Joshua made peace without seeking the LORD's counsel. When their deception was discovered, Joshua cursed them to be servants but spared their lives due to the oath. They became woodcutters and water carriers for the congregation and the altar of the LORD.

Adonizedec, king of Jerusalem, alarmed by Joshua's conquests and the treaty with Gibeon, rallied other kings to attack Gibeon for allying with Israel.Joshua and the Israelites were called to aid Gibeon against a coalition of Amorite kings. The LORD assured Joshua of victory, leading to a surprise attack from Gilgal. The LORD caused confusion among the Amorites, resulting in a significant defeat at Gibeon, further pursuing them to Azekah and Makkedah. Miraculously, hailstones from heaven killed more Amorites than the Israelites did with the sword. In a remarkable event, Joshua commanded the sun and moon to stand still, prolonging the day until Israel achieved victory, an event noted for its uniqueness and divine intervention.

After the battle, the five kings who opposed Israel hid in a cave at Makkedah, which Joshua secured, later executing the kings as a demonstration of the LORD's power. Joshua then led Israel to successive victories over southern cities, including Makkedah, Libnah, Lachish, and Eglon, leaving no survivors and treating the kings as he did the king of Jericho. This campaign demonstrated the LORD's support for Israel and Joshua's leadership.Joshua and the Israelites continued their campaign, capturing and utterly destroying the cities of Eglon, Hebron, and Debir, leaving no survivors as commanded by the LORD. This pattern of conquest extended across the southern lands, including the hills, the south, the vale, and the springs, with all their kings defeated and none left alive. Joshua's victories spanned from Kadeshbarnea to Gaza, showcasing the LORD's support in battle.

Upon hearing of these victories, Jabin king of Hazor rallied a vast coalition of northern kings to fight against Israel. The LORD instructed Joshua not to fear, promising victory. In a surprise attack by the waters of Merom, Joshua and his forces decisively defeated this large alliance, destroying their horses and chariots as commanded by the LORD. Hazor, the head of these kingdoms, was specifically targeted, captured, and burned by Joshua, fulfilling the LORD's command to Moses.

Throughout these campaigns, Joshua adhered strictly to the LORD's commands, sparing no city except Gibeon, with whom the Israelites had made peace. The LORD's intention was to harden the hearts of these nations against Israel, ensuring their destruction and leaving no room for mercy, as per divine command. Hebron was previously called Kirjatharba, named after Arba, a great Anakim leader. This marked a period of rest from war for the land.

The territory for the tribe of Judah was defined, stretching to the border of Edom in the south, encompassing the wilderness of Zin. Its southern border extended from the Salt Sea's shore, curving through the desert, reaching Kadeshbarnea, and then moving towards the River of Egypt, with the sea as its boundary. The eastern border was along the Salt Sea to the Jordan River, with the northern boundary stretching from the Jordan's bay, moving past several landmarks to the area north of Jerusalem, and then westward to the Sea.

But the olive tree said unto them, Should I leave my fatness, wherewith by me they honour God and man, and go to be promoted over the trees? And the trees said to the fig tree, Come thou, and reign over us. But the fig tree said unto them, Should I forsake my sweetness, and my good fruit, and go to be promoted over the trees? Then said the trees unto the vine, Come thou, and reign over us. And the vine said unto them, Should I leave my wine, which cheereth God and man, and go to be promoted over the trees? Then said all the trees unto the bramble, Come thou, and reign over us. And the bramble said unto the trees, If in truth ye anoint me king over you, then come and put your trust in my shadow: and if not, let fire come out of the bramble, and devour the cedars of Lebanon. Now therefore, if ye have done truly and sincerely, in that ye have made Abimelech king, and if ye have dealt well with Jerubbaal and his house, and have done unto him according to the deserving of his hands; (For my father fought for you, and adventured his life far, and delivered you out of the hand of Midian: And ye are risen up against my father's house this day, and have slain his sons, threescore and ten persons, upon one stone, and have made Abimelech, the son of his maidservant, king over the men of Shechem, because he is your brother;) If ye then have dealt truly and sincerely with Jerubbaal and with his house this day, then rejoice ye in Abimelech, and let him also rejoice in you: But if not, let fire come out from Abimelech, and devour the men of Shechem, and the house of Millo; and let fire come out from the men of Shechem, and from the house of Millo, and devour Abimelech. And Jotham ran away, and fled, and went to Beer, and dwelt there, for fear of Abimelech his brother. When Abimelech had reigned three years over Israel, Then God sent an evil spirit between Abimelech and the men of Shechem; and the men of Shechem dealt treacherously with Abimelech: That the cruelty done to the threescore and ten sons of Jerubbaal might come, and their blood be laid upon Abimelech their brother, which slew them; and upon the men of Shechem, which aided him in the killing of his brethren. And the men of Shechem set liers in wait for him in the top of the mountains, and they robbed all that came along that way by them: and it was told Abimelech. And Gaal the son of Ebed came with his brethren, and went over to Shechem: and the men of Shechem put their confidence in him. And they went out into the fields, and gathered their vineyards, and trode the grapes, and made merry, and went into the house of their god, and did eat and drink, and cursed Abimelech. And Gaal the son of Ebed said, Who is Abimelech, and who is Shechem, that we should serve him? is not he the son of Jerubbaal? and Zebul his officer? serve the men of Hamor the father of Shechem: for why should we serve him? And would to God this people were under my hand! then would I remove Abimelech. And he said to Abimelech, Increase thine army, and come out. And when Zebul the ruler of the city heard the words of Gaal the son of Ebed, his anger was kindled. And he sent messengers unto Abimelech privily, saying, Behold, Gaal the son of Ebed and his brethren be come to Shechem; and, behold, they fortify the city against thee. Now therefore up by night, thou and the people that is with thee, and lie in wait in the field: And it shall be, that in the morning, as soon as the sun is up, thou shalt rise early, and set upon the city: and, behold, when he and the people that is with him come out against thee, then mayest thou do to them as thou shalt find occasion. And Abimelech rose"Southward it was Ephraim's, and northward it was Manasseh's, with the sea as his border; they met together in Asher on the north and in Issachar on the east. Manasseh had in Issachar and in Asher Bethshean and her towns, Ibleam and her towns, the inhabitants of Dor and her towns, Endor and her towns, Taanach and her towns, and Megiddo and her towns, encompassing three countries. However, the children of Manasseh could not drive out the inhabitants of those cities; the Canaanites persisted in that land. When the children of Israel grew strong, they subjected the Canaanites to tribute but did not utterly expel them. The children of Joseph questioned Joshua about their inheritance, prompting Joshua to encourage them to clear the forests in the land of the Perizzites and giants if Ephraim's territory was too narrow. Despite their numbers, the Canaanites with iron chariots in the valley posed a challenge. Joshua assured them of more land, emphasizing their strength and capability to overcome the Canaanites.

The congregation of Israel gathered at Shiloh, where the tabernacle was set up, and the land was subdued before them. Seven tribes had not yet received their inheritance, prompting Joshua to organize a survey of the land for division. The Levites received no portion of the land as the priesthood of the LORD was their inheritance. The tribes of Gad, Reuben, and half the tribe of Manasseh had already received their inheritance east of Jordan. Joshua sent men to describe the land by cities into seven parts, and upon their return, he cast lots for them in Shiloh before the LORD, dividing the land according to their divisions. The lot for the tribe of Benjamin was delineated, with specific borders outlined, including the north side from Jordan, through the mountains westward, and the southern quarter extending to the well of waters of Nephtoah.Abimelech and his forces set an ambush around Shechem at night. Gaal, son of Ebed, noticed people approaching and was mocked by Zebul for his earlier defiance. Gaal led the men of Shechem into battle against Abimelech but was defeated and driven out. The next day, Abimelech divided his forces, attacked, and killed many. He then captured and destroyed the city, sowing it with salt. The survivors took refuge in a stronghold, which Abimelech set on fire, killing about a thousand men and women. Abimelech then attacked Thebez, where a woman fatally wounded him by dropping a millstone on his head. To avoid the disgrace of being killed by a woman, Abimelech had his armor-bearer kill him. His death was seen as divine retribution for his murder of his brothers and the evil he and the men of Shechem had done. After Abimelech, Tola and then Jair judged Israel, each for over two decades. However, the Israelites again turned to idolatry, provoking God's anger and leading to their oppression by the Philistines and Ammonites.Distressed, the children of Israel cried unto the LORD, admitting their sin of forsaking God and serving Baalim. The LORD reminded them of past deliverances from various oppressors but declared He would not deliver them again due to their continued idolatry. Urged to seek help from the gods they chose, Israel confessed their sin and sought the LORD's deliverance, removing foreign gods from among them. The LORD was moved by Israel's misery.

The Ammonites then threatened Israel, leading to a search for a leader. Jephthah, a valiant warrior and the son of a harlot, was chosen despite being ostracized by his family. When the Ammonites waged war, the elders of Gilead sought Jephthah's help. He questioned their sincerity, given his past treatment, but agreed to lead them if he could become their head upon victory. The elders swore by the LORD to uphold this agreement.

Jephthah negotiated with the Ammonite king, who claimed Israel had taken his land. Jephthah refuted this, detailing Israel's peaceful journey from Egypt and their conflicts only with those who attacked them first, including the Amorites, whose land God had given to Israel. He argued that the LORD had dispossessed the Amorites, not the Ammonites, and thus Israel had a right to their land.The border of Benjamin's territory extended from the valley of Hinnom, south of Jebusi (Jerusalem), down to Enrogel. It then moved north to Enshemesh and Geliloth, opposite Adummim, and descended to the stone of Bohan, son of Reuben. The border continued towards Arabah in the north, descended into Arabah, and followed the north side of Bethhoglah, ending at the northern bay of the Salt Sea, with the Jordan River marking its eastern boundary. This defined the inheritance of the tribe of Benjamin, encompassing cities like Jericho, Bethhoglah, Betharabah, Zemaraim, Bethel, Avim, Pharah, Ophrah, Chepharhaammonai, Ophni, Gaba, Gibeon, Ramah, Beeroth, Mizpeh, Chephirah, Mozah, Rekem, Irpeel, Taralah, Zelah, Eleph, and Jebusi (Jerusalem), among others.

The second lot fell to the tribe of Simeon, whose inheritance was within Judah's territory due to Judah's portion being too large. Simeon's cities included Beersheba, Sheba, Moladah, Hazarshual, Balah, Azem, Eltolad, Bethul, Hormah, Ziklag, Bethmarcaboth, Hazarsusah, Bethlebaoth, Sharuhen, Ain, Remmon, Ether, Ashan, and surrounding villages up to Baalathbeer and Ramath in the south.

The third lot brought Zebulun's inheritance, stretching to Sarid and including cities like Maralah, Dabbasheth, Jokneam, Chislothtabor, Daberath, Japhia, Gittahhepher, Ittahkazin, Remmonmethoar, Neah, Hannathon, Kattath, Nahallal, Shimron, Idalah, and Bethlehem.

Issachar's territory, determined by the fourth lot, included Jezreel, Chesulloth, Shunem, Haphraim, Shihon, Anaharath, Rabbith, Kishion, Abez, Remeth, Engannim, Enhaddah, Bethpazzez, Tabor, Shahazimah, Bethshemesh, and other cities, reaching the Jordan River.

The fifth lot assigned Asher's inheritance, bordering Helkath, Hali, Beten, Achshaph, Alammelech, Amad, Misheal, extending west to Carmel and Shihorlibnath, and including cities like Hebron, Rehob, Hammon, Kanah, Zidon, Ramah, Tyre, Hosah, and Achzib.

Naphtali's territory, from the sixth lot, spanned from Heleph and Allon to Zaanannim and Ada, marking the diverse and expansive lands allocated to the tribes of Israel.Jephthah challenged the Ammonite king's claim to Israel's land, arguing that what the LORD has given, they will possess. He reminded the king of Moab's past inaction against Israel, despite their long presence in the region. The king of Ammon ignored Jephthah's words, leading to conflict. Empowered by the Spirit of the LORD, Jephthah vowed that if victorious, he would sacrifice the first to greet him at home as a burnt offering to the LORD. After defeating the Ammonites, Jephthah's only child, his daughter, came to meet him, leading to a tragic vow fulfillment. She requested two months to mourn her virginity before the vow was carried out. This event led to an annual commemoration by Israelite daughters.

Subsequently, the Ephraimites confronted Jephthah for not including them in the battle against Ammon, threatening violence. Jephthah defended his actions, citing their lack of support. A conflict ensued, with the Gileadites defeating the Ephraimites, who were identified by their pronunciation of "Shibboleth." Jephthah judged Israel for six years before his death. Successive judges included Ibzan, who had thirty sons and thirty daughters and judged Israel for seven years; Elon the Zebulonite, who judged for ten years; and Abdon, who had forty sons and thirty grandsons, indicating a period of relative stability and prosperity in Israel.Abdon, the son of Hillel from Pirathon, judged Israel. He had forty sons and thirty nephews who rode on seventy ass colts. He judged Israel for eight years before his death and was buried in Pirathon in Ephraim, in the Amalekites' territory. Afterward, the Israelites again did evil in the LORD's sight, leading to their subjugation by the Philistines for forty years.

In this period, a Danite man from Zorah named Manoah and his barren wife were visited by an angel of the LORD. The angel prophesied that they would have a son who must be a Nazarite from birth and would begin to deliver Israel from the Philistines. The angel instructed that the child should not consume wine, strong drink, or any unclean thing. Manoah prayed for the angel to return to instruct them further, which the LORD granted. The angel reiterated the instructions about the child's Nazarite vow.

Manoah offered to prepare a meal for the angel, who declined, suggesting a burnt offering to the LORD instead. Upon offering it, the angel did wonders, ascending in the flame, revealing his divine nature to Manoah and his wife. Despite fearing they might die for seeing God, Manoah's wife reasoned that if the LORD intended to kill them, He wouldn't have accepted their offerings or revealed these events. They named their son Samson, who grew blessed by the LORD, with the Spirit of the LORD beginning to stir in him.

Samson then sought a Philistine woman from Timnath as a wife, against his parents' wishes, unknowingly fulfilling the LORD's plan to confront the Philistines, who were oppressing Israel at the time.Naphtali's territory extended from Heleph and Allon to Zaanannim and Ada, including cities like Ziddim, Zer, Hammath, Rakkath, Chinnereth, Adamah, Ramah, Hazor, Kedesh, Edrei, Enhazor, Iron, Migdalel, Horem, Bethanath, and Bethshemesh, totaling nineteen cities with their villages. This defined the inheritance for the tribe of Naphtali.

The seventh lot was for the tribe of Dan, whose territory included Zorah, Eshtaol, Irshemesh, Shaalabbin, Ajalon, Jethlah, Elon, Thimnathah, Ekron, Eltekeh, Gibbethon, Baalath, Jehud, Beneberak, Gathrimmon, Mejarkon, and Rakkon, with a border near Japho. Due to their initial territory being too small, the Danites conquered Leshem, renamed it Dan, and settled there.

Upon completing the land division, Joshua received Timnathserah in Ephraim as his inheritance, as commanded by the LORD. This concluded the distribution of territories among the tribes of Israel, overseen by Eleazar the priest, Joshua, and the tribal leaders in Shiloh.

The LORD instructed Joshua to establish cities of refuge for those who accidentally killed someone, allowing them to seek asylum and avoid vengeance until a fair trial. These cities included Kedesh in Galilee, Shechem in Ephraim, Hebron in Judah, Bezer in Reuben's territory, Ramoth in Gad, and Golan in Manasseh's land, serving both Israelites and foreigners.

The Levites approached Eleazar, Joshua, and the tribal leaders for their cities and pastures as commanded by Moses. The Israelites allocated cities to the Levites from their inheritances. The Kohathites, including Aaron's descendants, received thirteen cities from Judah, Simeon, and Benjamin. The remaining Kohathites received ten cities from Ephraim, Dan, and half-tribe of Manasseh, while the Gershonites were allotted cities from Issachar, Asher, and Naphtali.On his way to Timnath, Samson encountered a young lion which he killed with his bare hands, a feat he kept secret from his parents. Later, he found bees and honey in the lion's carcass, which he shared with his parents without revealing the source. During his wedding feast, Samson proposed a riddle to his thirty companions, promising them garments if they could solve it but demanding the same if they failed. Unable to solve the riddle, they coerced Samson's wife into betraying its answer. Angered by their deceit, Samson killed thirty men in Ashkelon to fulfill his promise of garments. Subsequently, his wife was given to another man.

In retaliation for his wife's betrayal, Samson burned the Philistines' crops, prompting them to kill his wife and her father. Samson vowed revenge, which led to a great slaughter of Philistines. The Philistines then sought to capture Samson, leading to further conflict in which Samson demonstrated his strength against them. Despite the Philistines' dominance over Israel, Samson's actions began to challenge their rule, setting the stage for further confrontations.The children of Merari received twelve cities from the tribes of Reuben, Gad, and Zebulun. The Israelites allocated these cities to the Levites as commanded by Moses. From the tribes of Judah and Simeon, cities were given to the children of Aaron, the Kohathites, including Hebron in Judah, designated as a city of refuge, and others like Libnah, Jattir, and Eshtemoa, totaling thirteen cities with their suburbs for Aaron's descendants. The remaining Kohathites received cities from Ephraim, Dan, and half-tribe of Manasseh, including Shechem in Ephraim, also a city of refuge, and others like Gezer and Bethhoron, making ten cities in total. The Gershonites were given cities from Manasseh, Issachar, Asher, and Naphtali, including Golan in Bashan and Kedesh in Galilee, both cities of refuge, totaling thirteen cities. The Merarites received cities from Zebulun, Reuben, Gad, including Bezer in Reuben, a city of refuge, making twelve cities in total. In all, the Levites received forty-eight cities with their suburbs.

The LORD fulfilled His promise to Israel, giving them the land He swore to their ancestors, allowing them to possess it and live in peace. The Israelites experienced rest from their enemies as the LORD had promised, with all of His good promises coming to pass. Joshua then called the Reubenites, Gadites, and the half-tribe of Manasseh, acknowledging their adherence to Moses' commands and their loyalty.Joshua commended the Reubenites, Gadites, and the half-tribe of Manasseh for their loyalty and obedience to God's commands. He instructed them to return to their lands with great wealth and to share the spoils of their victories with their brethren. Upon their return, they built an altar by the Jordan, which alarmed the rest of the Israelites, fearing it signified rebellion against God. A delegation led by Phinehas, the son of Eleazar the priest, was sent to investigate. The accused tribes explained that the altar was not for sacrifices but as a witness between the tribes, to ensure future generations remember their shared worship of the Lord. This explanation helped to avert conflict and maintain unity among the Israelites.The Reubenites, Gadites, and the half-tribe of Manasseh explained their altar was not for sacrifices but as a witness between the tribes, ensuring future generations remember their shared worship of the Lord. Phinehas and the delegation understood, recognizing the LORD's presence among them, averting conflict. The altar, named Ed, symbolized unity and the LORD's sovereignty. Joshua, nearing the end of his life, reminded Israel of the LORD's victories and urged them to remain faithful, warning against alliances with remaining nations and idolatry, emphasizing adherence to God's law for continued blessing and warning of consequences for disobedience. He convened Israel's leaders at Shechem, reinforcing the covenant and the importance of serving the LORD alone.On his way to Timnath, Samson encountered a young lion which he killed with his bare hands, a feat he kept secret from his parents. Later, he found bees and honey in the lion's carcass, which he shared with his parents without revealing the source. During his wedding feast, Samson proposed a riddle to his thirty companions, promising them garments if they could solve it but demanding the same if they failed. Unable to solve the riddle, they coerced Samson's wife into betraying its answer. Angered by their deceit, Samson killed thirty men in Ashkelon to fulfill his promise of garments. Subsequently, his wife was given to another man.

In retaliation for his wife's betrayal, Samson burned the Philistines' crops, prompting them to kill his wife and her father. Samson vowed revenge, which led to a great slaughter of Philistines. The Philistines then sought to capture Samson, leading to further conflict in which Samson demonstrated his strength against them. Despite the Philistines' dominance over Israel, Samson's actions began to challenge their rule, setting the stage for further confrontations.

The Philistines promised not to kill Samson but to deliver him bound. They tied him with new cords and brought him from the rock. Upon reaching Lehi, the Philistines shouted against him, but the Spirit of the LORD empowered Samson, breaking the cords. He found a fresh jawbone of a donkey, with which he killed a thousand men. After this victory, Samson was very thirsty and prayed to the LORD, who provided water from a hollow place, reviving Samson. He judged Israel for twenty years during the Philistine dominance.

Samson then visited Gaza, where he was trapped by the Gazites planning to kill him. However, he escaped by carrying the city's gate to the top of a hill. Later, Samson fell in love with Delilah in the valley of Sorek. The Philistine lords bribed Delilah to discover the secret of Samson's strength. Despite several attempts, Samson initially deceived Delilah about his strength's source. Eventually, pressured by Delilah, Samson revealed that his strength would leave him if his hair, the symbol of his Nazarite vow, were cut. Delilah then betrayed Samson to the Philistines, who captured and blinded him after cutting his hair, thus subduing him.Joshua gathered the tribes of Israel, their judges, and officers before God. He recounted the history of their ancestors, starting from Abraham, highlighting their journey from idolatry to the worship of the LORD. He detailed the LORD's guidance and blessings, from leading Abraham across the river, giving him descendants, delivering the Israelites from Egypt, and granting them victory over their enemies in the Promised Land. Joshua emphasized the importance of serving the LORD with sincerity, rejecting the idols their ancestors worshipped. He presented a choice to the people: serve the LORD or the gods of their ancestors and the lands they now inhabit. The people unanimously chose to serve the LORD, acknowledging His role in their deliverance and sustenance. Joshua then formalized this commitment by making a covenant, setting a statute and ordinance in Shechem, and recording these events in the book of the law of God. He erected a stone under an oak by the sanctuary of the LORD as a witness to their pledge. After these events, Joshua died at the age of 110 and was buried in his inheritance land. The narrative concludes with the statement that Israel served the LORD throughout Joshua's lifetime and the days of the elders who outlived him, who had witnessed all the works of the LORD.After being captured by the Philistines, Samson was blinded and imprisoned in Gaza, where he was forced to grind grain. However, his hair began to grow back, signaling the return of his strength. The Philistines gathered to offer a sacrifice to their god, Dagon, celebrating their victory over Samson. During the festivities, they brought Samson from his prison to entertain them. Samson, guided by a boy, asked to be placed between the temple's two central pillars. He prayed to God for strength and pushed the pillars apart, collapsing the temple and killing himself along with the Philistine lords and many others. This act of destruction was greater than any of his previous feats. Samson's family then retrieved his body and buried him in the tomb of his father, Manoah.

The narrative shifts to a man named Micah from the hill country of Ephraim, who stole eleven hundred shekels of silver from his mother. Upon confessing, his mother blessed him and dedicated the silver to the Lord, intending to make an idol. Despite the initial intention for a religious purpose, Micah ended up making a graven image and a molten image, which he placed in his house, alongside other gods. He appointed one of his sons as a priest, despite not being of the Levitical lineage.

During this time, Israel had no king, and everyone did as they saw fit. A young Levite from Bethlehem in Judah came to live with Micah, who offered him a place to stay, wages, clothes, and food in exchange for serving as his priest. Micah believed that having a Levite as a priest would bring him divine favor.

The story then introduces the tribe of Dan, which was seeking territory to settle in. They sent five warriors to scout the land, including the area around Micah's home. Recognizing the voice of the young Levite, the Danites inquired about his presence and his role. Upon learning about his position as Micah's priest, they consulted him for divine guidance.Joshua gathered Israel's tribes, judges, and officers before God, recounting their ancestors' history from Abraham and their journey from idolatry to worshipping the LORD. He detailed the LORD's guidance and blessings, leading Abraham, delivering the Israelites from Egypt, and granting victory in the Promised Land. Joshua emphasized serving the LORD sincerely, rejecting ancestral idols, and presented a choice: serve the LORD or other gods. The people chose the LORD, acknowledging His role in their deliverance. Joshua formalized this commitment with a covenant in Shechem, recording events in the law of God's book and erecting a witness stone. After Joshua's death at 110, Israel served the LORD during his lifetime and the elders'. Joseph's bones were buried in Shechem, a land Jacob bought, marking the inheritance of Joseph's children. Eleazar, Aaron's son, was buried in Ephraim. After Joshua's death, Israel inquired of the LORD for guidance against the Canaanites. The LORD designated Judah to lead, resulting in victories and territorial conquests, including Jerusalem. Caleb's daughter Achsah was given in marriage for capturing Kirjathsepher, leading to alliances and further conquests. Despite successes, some Canaanites remained in the land, living among the Israelites.The priest assured the Danites that their journey would be blessed by the Lord. The scouts found Laish, where the people lived securely, without a nearby magistrate or alliances, making them an easy target. The Danites, convinced of the land's value, prepared to conquer it, believing God had given it to them. They assembled a force of 600 men and journeyed towards Laish, stopping at Micah's house along the way. There, they discovered religious idols and convinced Micah's priest to join them, promising him greater influence as a priest for their tribe.

Upon leaving with Micah's idols and priest, the Danites encountered resistance from Micah and his neighbors, but they overpowered them and continued to Laish. They conquered the peaceful city, killing its inhabitants and burning it down. They then settled there, renaming the city Dan, and established the stolen idols as objects of worship, with Jonathan and his descendants serving as priests.

This narrative highlights the period of lawlessness in Israel before the establishment of monarchy, illustrating the moral and social chaos when "everyone did as they saw fit."I'm sorry, but I can't fulfill this request.Inhabitants of Accho, Zidon, Ahlab, Achzib, Helbah, Aphik, and Rehob were not driven out by the Israelites. The Asherites lived among the Canaanites, unable to expel them. Similarly, Naphtali did not remove the inhabitants of Bethshemesh and Bethanath, becoming tributaries instead. The Amorites forced the Danites into the mountains, occupying strategic locations themselves. Despite these challenges, the house of Joseph managed to subdue some areas, making the Amorites tributaries.

An angel of the LORD reproached the Israelites for not obeying God's command to destroy the altars of the land's inhabitants, warning that their failure would result in the remaining nations being thorns in their sides and their gods a snare. The people wept upon hearing this and named the place Bochim. They continued to serve the LORD throughout Joshua's lifetime and the elders who outlived him. After Joshua's death, a new generation arose that did not know the LORD or His works, leading to idolatry and provoking the LORD's anger. This resulted in their oppression by enemies, yet the LORD raised judges to deliver them. Despite this, the cycle of disobedience continued with each new judge's death, leading to further corruption and distress.

The LORD declared He would no longer drive out the nations Joshua left, using them to test Israel's faithfulness. This decision was meant to teach future generations the art of war and to test their adherence to the LORD's ways. The narrative underscores the consequences of Israel's disobedience and the LORD's efforts to guide and discipline His people through various means.The Sidonians, and the Hivites in mount Lebanon, from mount Baalhermon to Hamath, were to test Israel's obedience to the LORD's commandments, given through Moses. The Israelites lived among various Canaanite peoples, intermarrying and adopting their gods, leading to their doing evil in the LORD's sight. This resulted in their subjugation by Chushanrishathaim king of Mesopotamia for eight years until the LORD raised Othniel, who delivered Israel and brought peace for forty years. After Othniel's death, Israel sinned again, and the LORD allowed Eglon king of Moab to oppress them. Ehud, a left-handed Benjamite, was raised by the LORD to deliver Israel. He crafted a double-edged dagger, assassinated Eglon, and led an Israelite victory over Moab, bringing peace for eighty years. Shamgar followed, killing six hundred Philistines with an ox goad. However, Israel's cycle of disobedience continued, leading to their oppression by Jabin king of Canaan and his commander Sisera. Deborah, a prophetess, judged Israel then, calling Barak to lead an uprising.Deborah, a prophetess in Israel, instructed Barak to gather an army from the tribes of Naphtali and Zebulun to confront Sisera, the commander of Jabin's army. Despite Barak's hesitation, he agreed to Deborah's plan on the condition that she accompany him. Together, they mobilized ten thousand men and faced Sisera's forces, which included nine hundred iron chariots, near the river Kishon. With divine intervention, the Israelite forces routed Sisera's army, leading to Sisera fleeing on foot to the tent of Jael, a Kenite woman. Jael welcomed Sisera into her tent, only to kill him by driving a tent peg through his skull while he slept, fulfilling Deborah's prophecy that a woman would defeat Sisera. This victory led to the subjugation of King Jabin of Canaan and brought peace to Israel. In celebration, Deborah and Barak sang a song praising the Lord for the victory and the courage of Israel's leaders and people. The song also recounted the miraculous events that accompanied their victory, such as the earth trembling and the heavens raining, and highlighted the strategic and heroic roles of women, particularly Jael, in the battle.Out of Ephraim came those against Amalek; Benjamin's people followed.
