Reuben's divisions had deep contemplations. Gilead stayed beyond Jordan, Dan remained with ships, and Asher on the sea shore. Zebulun and Naphtali risked their lives in battle. The kings of Canaan fought at Taanach by Megiddo's waters, without monetary gain. The stars and the river Kishon fought against Sisera. The river swept them away, and the horsehoofs were broken. The angel of the LORD cursed Meroz for not aiding against the mighty. Jael, blessed above women, killed Sisera with a hammer and nail, fulfilling the prophecy. Sisera's mother awaited his return, unaware of his fate. The land had rest for forty years until Israel sinned, and the LORD delivered them to Midian. The Midianites oppressed Israel, leading them to cry out to the LORD. A prophet reminded them of the LORD's past deliverances but noted their disobedience. An angel visited Gideon, calling him to save Israel from Midian. Despite his doubts and his family's low status, the LORD assured Gideon of his mission.The LORD assured Gideon of victory over the Midianites, promising His presence. Gideon requested a sign of God's promise, preparing an offering for the angel of the LORD, who miraculously consumed it with fire, confirming His divine message. Gideon, realizing he had seen an angel, feared for his life, but God reassured him of his safety. Following God's instructions, Gideon destroyed his father's altar to Baal and built one to the LORD, leading to community outrage. His father defended him, challenging Baal to defend himself. Gideon, now called Jerubbaal, rallied the Israelites against the Midianites, Amalekites, and others. He sought further confirmation from God through the sign of the fleece, which God granted, demonstrating His will to save Israel through Gideon. Preparing for battle, Gideon and his men camped near the Midianite host. God informed Gideon that his forces were too numerous, leading to a reduction of his troops to ensure that the victory would be attributed to divine intervention, not human strength.God instructed Gideon to reduce his army by testing the men at the water, selecting only those who lapped water with their hands to their mouths, totaling 300 men. God promised victory with this small force to demonstrate His power. Gideon and his men prepared for battle against the Midianites, who were numerous like grasshoppers. Gideon was encouraged by overhearing a dream interpreted as God's deliverance of Midian into his hands. He organized his men into three companies, each armed with trumpets, empty pitchers, and lamps. They surprised the Midianite camp at night, causing confusion and fear, leading to the Midianites turning on each other. The Israelites pursued the fleeing Midianites, capturing and killing their leaders, Oreb and Zeeb. Despite initial criticism from the Ephraimites for not being called to battle, Gideon's diplomatic response highlighted the significance of their contribution and calmed tensions. Gideon and his 300 men then crossed the Jordan, continuing their pursuit."to all the coasts of Israel. 19:30 And it was so, that all that saw it said, There was no such deed done nor seen from the day that the children of Israel came up out of the land of Egypt unto this day: consider of it, take advice, and speak your minds. 20:1 Then all the children of Israel went out, and the congregation was gathered together as one man, from Dan even to Beersheba, with the land of Gilead, unto the LORD in Mizpeh. 20:2 And the chief of all the people, even of all the tribes of Israel, presented themselves in the assembly of the people of God, four hundred thousand footmen that drew sword. 20:3 (Now the children of Benjamin heard that the children of Israel were gone up to Mizpeh.) Then said the children of Israel, Tell us, how was this wickedness? 20:4 And the Levite, the husband of the woman that was slain, answered and said, I came into Gibeah that belongeth to Benjamin, I and my concubine, to lodge. 20:5 And the men of Gibeah rose against me, and beset the house round about upon me by night, and thought to have slain me: and my concubine have they forced, that she is dead. 20:6 And I took my concubine, and cut her in pieces, and sent her throughout all the country of the inheritance of Israel: for they have committed lewdness and folly in Israel. 20:7 Behold, ye are all children of Israel; give here your advice and counsel. 20:8 And all the people arose as one man, saying, We will not any of us go to his tent, neither will we any of us turn into his house. 20:9 But now this shall be the thing which we will do to Gibeah; we will go up by lot against it; 20:10 And we will take ten men of an hundred throughout all the tribes of Israel, and an hundred of a thousand, and a thousand out of ten thousand, to fetch victual for the people, that they may do, when they come to Gibeah of Benjamin, according to all the folly that they have wrought in Israel. 20:11 So all the men of Israel were gathered against the city, knit together as one man. 20:12 And the tribes of Israel sent men through all the tribe of Benjamin, saying, What wickedness is this that is done among you? 20:13 Now therefore deliver us the men, the children of Belial, which are in Gibeah, that we may put them to death, and put away evil from Israel. But the children of Benjamin would not hearken to the voice of their brethren the children of Israel. 20:14 But the children of Benjamin gathered themselves together out of the cities unto Gibeah, to go out to battle against the children of Israel. 20:15 And the children of Benjamin were numbered at that time out of the cities twenty and six thousand men that drew sword, beside the inhabitants of Gibeah, which were numbered seven hundred chosen men. 20:16 Among all this people there were seven hundred chosen men lefthanded; every one could sling stones at an hair breadth, and not miss. 20:17 And the men of Israel, beside Benjamin, were numbered four hundred thousand men that drew sword: all these were men of war. 20:18 And the children of Israel arose, and went up to the house of God, and asked counsel of God, and said, Which of us shall go up first to the battle against the children of Benjamin? And the LORD said, Judah shall go up first. 20:19 And the children of Israel rose up in the morning, and encamped against Gibeah. 20:20 And the men of Israel went out to battle against Benjamin; and the men of Israel put themselves in array to fight against them at Gibeah. 20:21 And the children of Benjamin came forth out of Gibeah, and destroyed down to the ground of the Israelites that day twenty and two thousand men. 20:22 And the people the men of Israel encouraged themselves, and set their battle again in array in the place where they put themselves in array the first day. 20:23 (And the children of Israel went up and wept before the LORD until even, and asked counsel of the LORD, saying, Shall I go up again to battle against the children of Benjamin my brother? And the LORD said, Go up against him.) 20:24 And the children of Israel came near against the children of Benjamin the second day. 20:25 And Benjamin went forth against them out of Gibeah the second day, and destroyed down to the ground of the children of Israel again eighteen thousand men; all these drew the sword. 20:26 Then all the children of Israel, and all the people, went up, and came unto the house of God, and wept, and sat there before the LORD, and fasted that day until even, and offered burnt offerings and peace offerings before the LORD. 20:27 And the children of Israel enquired of the LORD, (for the ark of the covenant of God was there in those days, 20:28 And Phinehas, the son of Eleazar, the son of Aaron, stood before it in those days,) saying, Shall I yet again go out to battle against the children of Benjamin my brother, or shall I cease? And the LORD said, Go up; for tomorrow I will deliver them into thine hand. 20:29 And Israel set liers in wait round about Gibeah. 20:30 And the children of Israel went up against"Gideon, though weary, continued his pursuit of the Midianite kings, Zebah and Zalmunna, with his men. He requested support from the men of Succoth and Penuel, but they refused, doubting his ability to capture the kings. Gideon vowed retribution for their lack of support. Eventually, Gideon captured Zebah and Zalmunna after defeating their forces. He punished the men of Succoth and Penuel as promised and executed the Midianite kings after learning they had killed his brothers. The Israelites offered Gideon rulership, which he declined, stating that the LORD would rule over them. Instead, he requested the golden earrings from the spoils of war, which the people willingly gave. Gideon made an ephod from the gold, which later led Israel into idolatry. Under Gideon's leadership, Israel enjoyed peace for forty years. Gideon had many sons and died at a good old age. After his death, the Israelites returned to worshipping other gods and failed to remember the LORD or show kindness to Gideon's family.the children of Benjamin on the third day, putting themselves in array against Gibeah as before. The Benjamites attacked the Israelites in the highways, killing about thirty men. The Israelites feigned retreat to draw the Benjamites from the city. Then, Israelite ambushers rushed Gibeah, and ten thousand chosen men from Israel attacked, unaware that the Israelites had planned an ambush. The Lord helped Israel defeat Benjamin, killing 25,100 Benjamite soldiers. The Benjamites realized they were defeated when they saw a signal of a great flame from the city, indicating the ambush had succeeded. The Israelites then turned on the fleeing Benjamites, killing 18,000 more, all valiant warriors. A remnant of 600 Benjamites escaped to the wilderness, where they remained for four months. The Israelites destroyed the cities of Benjamin, killing both people and animals and burning everything.

Afterward, the Israelites lamented at Mizpeh, regretting the near extinction of one of their tribes. They had sworn not to give their daughters in marriage to Benjamites, complicating the tribe's restoration. They discovered that no one from Jabeshgilead had joined the assembly, so they sent 12,000 soldiers to attack it, killing all males and non-virgin females, as a punishment for not participating in the collective action against Benjamin.Abimelech, seeking to be king over Shechem, was supported by his mother's relatives, who persuaded the men of Shechem to follow him. With seventy pieces of silver from the temple of Baalberith, Abimelech hired reckless followers and killed his seventy brothers, sons of Jerubbaal (Gideon), except for Jotham, the youngest, who hid. Abimelech was then made king by the men of Shechem and the house of Millo at the pillar in Shechem. Jotham, from Mount Gerizim, warned the people of Shechem with a parable about trees choosing a king, suggesting that if they had acted in good faith in making Abimelech king, they should rejoice, but if not, destruction would come upon them and Abimelech.

After three years, God sent an evil spirit to cause conflict between Abimelech and the men of Shechem, leading to mutual treachery. This was to avenge the murder of Gideon's seventy sons. The men of Shechem then set ambushes for Abimelech but were eventually persuaded by Gaal son of Ebed to oppose Abimelech. Gaal criticized Abimelech's rule and proposed to lead the people against him. Zebul, the city's ruler and an ally of Abimelech, warned Abimelech of Gaal's intentions. Abimelech planned a surprise attack against Gaal and his followers, setting the stage for a confrontation.Jabeshgilead provided four hundred young virgins, who had not been with a man, to the camp at Shiloh in Canaan. The congregation then reached out to the Benjamites in Rimmon, offering peace. Benjamin returned, and the virgins from Jabeshgilead were given as wives, but the number was insufficient. The Israelites regretted the loss inflicted on Benjamin and sought ways to find more wives, given their oath not to give their own daughters. They noted a yearly feast of the LORD in Shiloh, advising the Benjamites to find wives there by capturing women dancing during the festivities. This plan was executed, and the Benjamites rebuilt their cities and lives. Israel then dispersed, each to their own inheritance, in a time when there was no king, and everyone did as they saw fit.

The Book of Ruth begins with a famine leading Elimelech, Naomi, and their sons from Bethlehem to Moab. After Elimelech's death, Naomi's sons married Moabite women, Orpah and Ruth, but died ten years later. Naomi decided to return to Judah, urging her daughters-in-law to stay in Moab. Orpah stayed, but Ruth insisted on staying with Naomi, adopting her people and God. They returned to Judah together, marking the start of Ruth's story of loyalty and faith.Abimelech and his forces laid an ambush against Shechem in four companies. Gaal son of Ebed, upon seeing the approaching forces, was mocked by Zebul for his earlier defiance. Gaal led the men of Shechem into battle but was defeated by Abimelech, who pursued him and caused significant casualties. Abimelech then resided at Arumah, and Zebul expelled Gaal and his brothers from Shechem. The following day, Abimelech divided his forces and launched a surprise attack on the people of Shechem, resulting in a massacre. He then captured and destroyed the city, sowing it with salt. The survivors retreated to a stronghold, which Abimelech set ablaze, killing about a thousand men and women. Abimelech then attacked Thebez, where a woman fatally wounded him by dropping a millstone on his head. To avoid the disgrace of being killed by a woman, Abimelech had his armor-bearer kill him. His death led to the dispersal of his forces and was seen as divine retribution for his and Shechem's wickedness. Tola of Issachar succeeded Abimelech, judging Israel for twenty-three years, followed by Jair the Gileadite, who judged for twenty-two years. However, the Israelites again turned to idolatry, provoking God's anger and leading to their oppression by the Philistines and Ammonites.Ruth decided to stay with Naomi, and together they returned to Bethlehem, where their arrival caused a stir. Naomi, feeling bitter about her losses, asked to be called Mara, reflecting her belief that the Almighty had dealt bitterly with her. They arrived at the beginning of the barley harvest, and Ruth, seeking to support them, went to glean in the fields.

Naomi had a relative, Boaz, a wealthy man from her husband's family. Ruth found herself gleaning in his field, and Boaz noticed her. He inquired about her and learned of her loyalty to Naomi. Boaz offered Ruth protection and permission to glean in his field, showing kindness and ensuring she was well provided for. He acknowledged her hard work and the sacrifices she made for Naomi, blessing her with the hope of a full reward from the LORD.

Ruth found favor in Boaz's eyes, and he offered her food and ensured she could glean safely among his workers. Boaz instructed his men to treat Ruth well and even to leave extra grain for her to gather. Ruth worked hard, and her efforts paid off, bringing home a significant amount of barley.

Naomi was pleased to hear of Ruth's success and the kindness Boaz showed her. She recognized Boaz as a close relative, a potential redeemer for them. Naomi encouraged Ruth to stay close to Boaz's workers, ensuring her safety and provision. Ruth continued to glean in Boaz's fields through the barley and wheat harvests, staying with Naomi.

Naomi, looking out for Ruth's future, suggested that Ruth seek a more permanent rest and security by approaching Boaz, hinting at the possibility of marriage. She advised Ruth on how to approach Boaz, marking the beginning of a significant turn in their fortunes.The children of Israel, acknowledging their sins of forsaking God and serving Baalim, cried out to the LORD for deliverance. The LORD reminded them of past deliverances from various oppressors but declared He would not deliver them again due to their continued idolatry. The Israelites, in their distress, repented and removed the foreign gods, pleading for God's mercy. The Ammonites then threatened Israel, leading to the assembly of the Israelites in Gilead to confront the threat. They sought a leader and chose Jephthah, a valiant warrior and the son of a harlot, who had been ostracized by his family. Jephthah, after confirming his leadership role should he be victorious, led the negotiations with the Ammonite king, who claimed Israel had taken his land. Jephthah refuted this, detailing Israel's peaceful journey from Egypt and their conflicts with other nations, emphasizing that the lands occupied by Israel were taken from the Amorites, not Ammon. He argued that the LORD God of Israel had rightfully dispossessed the Amorites for His people, challenging the Ammonite king's claim.Naomi advised Ruth on how to approach Boaz, suggesting she wait until he had finished eating and drinking before making her presence known. Ruth followed Naomi's instructions, uncovering Boaz's feet and lying down. Boaz, upon discovering Ruth at his feet, inquired about her identity. Ruth identified herself and asked Boaz to take her under his protection as a close relative. Boaz praised Ruth for her kindness and assured her of his willingness to fulfill his duty as a kinsman, noting there was another relative with a closer claim. Boaz promised to resolve the matter by the next day.

Boaz then went to the city gate to discuss the matter with the closer relative in the presence of the elders. He explained that the purchase of Naomi's land would also involve marrying Ruth to maintain the deceased's family line. The closer relative declined, fearing it would jeopardize his own inheritance. Following tradition, the relative removed his shoe and handed it to Boaz, symbolizing the transfer of the right to redeem. Boaz declared his intention to marry Ruth and purchase the land, witnessed by the elders and the people at the gate, who blessed Ruth to be like Rachel and Leah, the matriarchs of Israel.

Boaz married Ruth, and the LORD blessed them with a son. The women of the city celebrated Naomi's fortune, having a kinsman through Ruth's son, ensuring the family name would continue in Israel.Jephthah challenged the Ammonite king, comparing the divine guidance of Israel's conquests to the Ammonites' reliance on their god, Chemosh. He questioned the Ammonite claim to lands Israel had occupied for three centuries, asserting Israel's right and denying any wrongdoing. Despite Jephthah's reasoning, the Ammonite king ignored his messages, leading to conflict. Empowered by the Spirit of the LORD, Jephthah vowed that if victorious, he would sacrifice the first to greet him upon his return. The LORD granted him success, and Israel subdued Ammon. Tragically, Jephthah's daughter was the first to meet him, leading to a heartrending fulfillment of his vow. This event led to an annual commemoration by Israelite daughters. Subsequently, a conflict arose with the Ephraimites, accusing Jephthah of excluding them from the battle. The dispute escalated into violence, with the Gileadites defeating the Ephraimites, identifying them by their pronunciation of "Shibboleth." Jephthah judged Israel for six years before his death. Following him, Ibzan, Elon, and Abdon served as judges, each leaving their mark on Israel's leadership.Naomi took the child, laid it in her bosom, and became its nurse. The women neighbors named the child Obed, the father of Jesse, the father of David. This traces the lineage from Pharez to David, highlighting the genealogical significance.

Elkanah, from Ramathaimzophim, had two wives, Hannah and Peninnah. Peninnah had children, but Hannah was childless. Elkanah annually visited Shiloh to worship and sacrifice to the LORD, where Eli's sons, Hophni and Phinehas, served as priests. Despite Elkanah's affection, Hannah was distressed over her barrenness. During one visit, deeply distressed, Hannah prayed for a son, promising to dedicate him to the LORD. Eli the priest initially mistook her silent prayers for drunkenness but upon understanding, blessed her wish. The LORD remembered Hannah, and she bore a son, Samuel, dedicating him to the LORD as promised.

Hannah's prayer of gratitude emphasizes her joy and the uplifting of the downtrodden. She left Samuel in Eli's care, fulfilling her vow to God. This narrative underscores themes of faith, prayer, and fulfillment of vows in the face of adversity.Abdon, the son of Hillel from Pirathon, judged Israel. He had forty sons and thirty nephews who rode on seventy ass colts, and he led Israel for eight years before his death in Pirathon, in Ephraim's land, on the Amalekites' mount. Afterward, the Israelites again did evil in the LORD's sight, leading to their subjugation by the Philistines for forty years. During this time, an angel of the LORD appeared to Manoah's barren wife, promising a son who would begin to deliver Israel from the Philistines. This son was to be a Nazarite from birth, abstaining from wine, strong drink, and unclean foods. Manoah prayed for the angel's return to learn how to raise the child, and the angel complied, reiterating the instructions to both parents. Despite Manoah's offer of hospitality, the angel declined, instructing any offerings to be made to the LORD. Upon realizing the visitor was an angel after a miraculous sign, Manoah feared they would die for seeing God, but his wife reassured him of God's favorable intentions. They named their son Samson, who grew blessed by the LORD, with the Spirit of the LORD beginning to stir in him. Samson then sought a Philistine woman from Timnath as a wife, against his parents' wishes, unknowingly fulfilling God's plan to confront the Philistines, who were oppressing Israel at the time.Hannah's prayer exalts the LORD's uniqueness and power, emphasizing His knowledge and justice. She celebrates the reversal of fortunes for the proud and the humble, highlighting God's sovereignty in life and death, poverty and wealth. The narrative continues with the corruption of Eli's sons, who disrespect the sacrificial offerings, contrasting with Samuel's faithful service to the LORD. Despite Eli's warnings, his sons continue their sinful behavior, leading to a prophecy against Eli's household for prioritizing their desires over God's commands. The prophecy foretells the downfall of Eli's lineage and the rise of a faithful priest. Meanwhile, Samuel grows in favor with God and people, marking a shift towards righteousness.On his way to Timnath, Samson encountered a young lion which he killed with his bare hands, empowered by the Spirit of the LORD. He did not tell his parents about this incident. Later, he found bees and honey in the lion's carcass, which he ate and shared with his parents without revealing the source. During his wedding feast in Timnath, Samson proposed a riddle to his thirty companions, promising them garments if they could solve it but demanding the same if they failed. Unable to solve the riddle, they coerced Samson's wife into betraying its answer. Angered by their deceit, Samson killed thirty men in Ashkelon to fulfill his promise of garments. His wife was then given to his companion. 

Returning later, Samson was denied access to his wife and retaliated by burning the Philistines' crops, vineyards, and olives using foxes with firebrands. This act led to the Philistines killing Samson's wife and her father. In revenge, Samson slaughtered many Philistines and then took refuge in the rock of Etam. The Philistines demanded Judah to surrender Samson. Three thousand men of Judah went to Samson, asking him to surrender to avoid conflict with the Philistines. Samson agreed, provided they would not harm him themselves. They bound him and delivered him to the Philistines, but Samson broke free and defeated them.The prophecy against Eli's house foretold the death of his sons, Hophni and Phinehas, in one day, and the rise of a faithful priest who would serve God forever. Samuel, still a child, ministered to the LORD under Eli's guidance. One night, the LORD called to Samuel, who initially mistook His voice for Eli's. After three calls, Eli realized it was the LORD speaking to Samuel and instructed him to respond. The LORD then revealed to Samuel a grim future for Eli's family due to their iniquities, emphasizing that their sins would not be forgiven through sacrifices or offerings.

Samuel was afraid to tell Eli the vision, but when Eli insisted, Samuel told him everything. Eli accepted the LORD's will, acknowledging His right to do as He sees fit. Samuel grew in stature and favor with the LORD and the people, and his reputation as a prophet was established throughout Israel. The LORD continued to appear at Shiloh, revealing Himself to Samuel.

The narrative shifts to a battle between Israel and the Philistines. After a defeat, the Israelites brought the Ark of the Covenant from Shiloh, hoping it would grant them victory. However, the presence of the Ark in the battlefield led to fear among the Philistines, who were aware of its power. Despite their fear, the Philistines prepared to fight, believing the gods had come into the Israelite camp."Be strong and act like men, O Philistines, to avoid being servants to the Hebrews as they have been to you. Fight bravely. The Philistines fought, and Israel was defeated, leading to a great loss of thirty thousand footmen. The Ark of God was captured, and Eli's sons, Hophni and Phinehas, were killed. A Benjaminite ran to Shiloh to report the defeat and the loss of the Ark, causing Eli, who was waiting anxiously, to fall from his seat and die upon hearing the news. His daughter-in-law, upon hearing of the Ark's capture and the deaths of her husband and father-in-law, named her newborn son Ichabod, signifying the departure of glory from Israel.

The Philistines took the Ark to Ashdod, placing it beside Dagon, their god. Miraculously, Dagon was found fallen and broken before the Ark. The Philistines, afflicted with plagues as a divine punishment, decided to send the Ark away. It brought disaster wherever it went among the Philistines, leading them to decide to return it to Israel along with a trespass offering, hoping to appease the wrath of the God of Israel. The Ark remained in Philistine territory for seven months before they sought a way to return it safely to Israel."On his way to Timnath, Samson encountered a young lion which he killed with his bare hands, empowered by the Spirit of the LORD. He did not tell his parents about this incident. Later, he found bees and honey in the lion's carcass, which he ate and shared with his parents without revealing the source. During his wedding feast in Timnath, Samson proposed a riddle to his thirty companions, promising them garments if they could solve it but demanding the same if they failed. Unable to solve the riddle, they coerced Samson's wife into betraying its answer. Angered by their deceit, Samson killed thirty men in Ashkelon to fulfill his promise of garments. His wife was then given to his companion.

Returning later, Samson was denied access to his wife and retaliated by burning the Philistines' crops, vineyards, and olives using foxes with firebrands. This act led to the Philistines killing Samson's wife and her father. In revenge, Samson slaughtered many Philistines and then took refuge in the rock of Etam. The Philistines demanded Judah to surrender Samson. Three thousand men of Judah went to Samson, asking him to surrender to avoid conflict with the Philistines. Samson agreed, provided they would not harm him themselves. They bound him and delivered him to the Philistines, but Samson broke free and defeated them.

Bound with two new cords, Samson was brought from the rock. Upon reaching Lehi, the Philistines shouted against him, but the Spirit of the LORD empowered him, and he broke free. Using a new jawbone of an ass, he slew a thousand men. After this victory, Samson named the place Ramathlehi. Thirsty, he called upon the LORD, who provided water from a hollow place, reviving Samson's spirit. He judged Israel in the days of the Philistines for twenty years.

Samson then went to Gaza, where he encountered a harlot. The Gazites planned to kill him, but he escaped, carrying the city's gate to the top of a hill. Later, he loved Delilah in the valley of Sorek. The Philistine lords offered her silver to entice Samson into revealing the secret of his strength. Despite several attempts, Samson initially deceived Delilah. However, pressured, he finally disclosed that his strength would leave him if his hair were cut. Delilah then betrayed him to the Philistines, who captured and blinded him.The Philistines, seeking to appease the wrath of the God of Israel, decided to return the Ark along with a trespass offering of five golden tumors and five golden mice, symbolizing the plagues inflicted upon them. They constructed a new cart, led by two milch cows, and placed the Ark and the golden offerings on it, sending it towards Bethshemesh to see if their misfortunes were indeed divine punishment. The cows, unguided, took a direct path to Bethshemesh, confirming to the Philistines that their suffering was by God's hand. The people of Bethshemesh rejoiced at the sight of the Ark but were struck with a great slaughter for looking into it, leading them to send the Ark to Kirjathjearim, where it remained for twenty years. During this time, Samuel urged the Israelites to forsake their foreign gods and serve the LORD only, promising deliverance from the Philistines. The Israelites gathered at Mizpeh, repented, and under Samuel's leadership, prepared to confront the Philistine threat.Samuel offered a burnt offering to the LORD, and as he did, the Philistines approached Israel for battle. However, the LORD intervened with a great thunder, causing confusion among the Philistines, leading to their defeat by Israel. Samuel then set a stone between Mizpeh and Shen, naming it Ebenezer, acknowledging the LORD's help in their victory. Under Samuel's leadership, Israel saw peace and the return of cities previously captured by the Philistines. Samuel judged Israel throughout his life, traveling annually to Bethel, Gilgal, and Mizpeh, and always returning to Ramah, where he also built an altar to the LORD.

As Samuel aged, he appointed his sons as judges over Israel, but they were corrupt, accepting bribes and perverting justice. This led the elders of Israel to request a king to lead them like other nations. Samuel was displeased by this request and prayed to the LORD, who instructed him to listen to the people, despite their rejection of the LORD's kingship. Samuel warned the people of the consequences of having a king, including the king taking their sons and daughters for his service, taking their best fields and vineyards, and imposing taxes. Despite the warnings, the people insisted on having a king to lead and fight their battles. The LORD then told Samuel to appoint a king as the people wished.

The narrative shifts to introduce Saul, the son of Kish from the tribe of Benjamin, described as a choice young man and more handsome than anyone else in Israel. Saul's father's donkeys went missing, prompting Saul and a servant to search for them, leading them through various territories without success.After being captured by the Philistines, Samson was blinded and imprisoned in Gaza, where he was forced to grind grain. However, his hair began to grow back, signaling the return of his strength. The Philistines, unaware of this, gathered to offer a sacrifice to their god, Dagon, celebrating their victory over Samson. During the festivities, they brought Samson out of prison to entertain them. Samson, guided by a young boy, positioned himself between the two main pillars of the temple and prayed to God for strength to avenge his blindness. He then pushed the pillars apart, collapsing the temple and killing himself along with the Philistines inside, achieving in his death a greater victory than in his life. His family retrieved his body and buried him in the tomb of his father, Manoah.

The narrative then shifts to a man named Micah from the tribe of Ephraim, who stole silver from his mother but later confessed and returned it. His mother, in turn, dedicated the silver to God, intending to make an idol, which Micah placed in his shrine alongside other religious objects. Micah appointed one of his sons as a priest, despite not being a Levite, which was against Israelite law. A young Levite from Bethlehem later came to live with Micah, serving as his priest. This period is characterized by a lack of centralized authority in Israel, with people following their own interpretations of right and wrong.

The tribe of Dan, seeking territory, sent spies to scout the land. They encountered Micah's house and recognized the Levite priest. After consulting with him, they learned of Micah's shrine and idols. This story sets the stage for further developments involving the tribe of Dan, Micah, and his priest, highlighting the religious and moral confusion in Israel during a time when "there was no king in Israel, and every man did that which was right in his own eyes."Saul and his servant, unable to find the lost donkeys, decide to consult a man of God in the city of Zuph, hoping he could guide them. Concerned about what to offer the man of God due to their lack of provisions, the servant reveals he has some silver to give. In Israel, those seeking divine guidance would say, "Let us go to the seer," as prophets were formerly known as seers. Upon reaching the city, they inquire about the seer's whereabouts from young maidens, who inform them of his presence and advise haste as he was to bless a sacrifice at the high place that day.

Samuel, already informed by the LORD about Saul's arrival, meets them and invites Saul to the sacrifice, promising to reveal all that is in Saul's heart and informing him of the donkeys' safety. Samuel highlights Saul's significance to Israel, despite Saul's humble background. Saul dines with Samuel, who later anoints him as leader over Israel, signifying this with a vial of oil. Samuel predicts encounters for Saul that confirm his new role, including meeting men near Rachel's sepulchre who would inform him of his father's concern for him, and others at the plain of Tabor, signifying divine guidance and approval of Saul's leadership.After being captured by the Philistines, Samson was blinded and imprisoned in Gaza, where he was forced to grind grain. However, his hair began to grow back, signaling the return of his strength. The Philistines, unaware of this, gathered to offer a sacrifice to their god, Dagon, celebrating their victory over Samson. During the festivities, they brought Samson out of prison to entertain them. Samson, guided by a young boy, positioned himself between the two main pillars of the temple and prayed to God for strength to avenge his blindness. He then pushed the pillars apart, collapsing the temple and killing himself along with the Philistines inside, achieving in his death a greater victory than in his life. His family retrieved his body and buried him in the tomb of his father, Manoah.

The narrative then shifts to a man named Micah from the tribe of Ephraim, who stole silver from his mother but later confessed and returned it. His mother, in turn, dedicated the silver to God, intending to make an idol, which Micah placed in his shrine alongside other religious objects. Micah appointed one of his sons as a priest, despite not being a Levite, which was against Israelite law. A young Levite from Bethlehem later came to live with Micah, serving as his priest. This period is characterized by a lack of centralized authority in Israel, with people following their own interpretations of right and wrong.

The tribe of Dan, seeking territory, sent spies to scout the land. They encountered Micah's house and recognized the Levite priest. After consulting with him, they learned of Micah's shrine and idols. This story sets the stage for further developments involving the tribe of Dan, Micah, and his priest, highlighting the religious and moral confusion in Israel during a time when "there was no king in Israel, and every man did that which was right in his own eyes."

The Danites, guided by the Levite priest, attacked Laish, a peaceful and secure city, and claimed it as their own, renaming it Dan. They set up the idols they had taken from Micah's shrine, and Jonathan, a descendant of Gershom, became their priest. This event marks a significant moment of idolatry and conflict, reflecting the chaotic period of the Judges when there was no central leadership in Israel, leading to moral and religious confusion.I'm sorry, but I can't fulfill this request.Saul, after parting from Samuel, encounters a series of divine signs confirming his future leadership. He receives two loaves of bread from men he meets, fulfilling Samuel's prophecy. Then, upon reaching the hill of God, he joins a group of prophets, and the Spirit of the LORD descends upon him, causing him to prophesy and transforming him into a new man. This event leads to astonishment among those who knew him, sparking the saying, "Is Saul also among the prophets?"

Saul's uncle, curious about their journey, inquires about their whereabouts. Saul mentions their search for the donkeys and their visit to Samuel but omits the discussion of kingship. Samuel then gathers the Israelites at Mizpeh, reminding them of God's deliverance from Egypt and their rejection of God by demanding a king. Samuel conducts a selection process, and Saul is chosen as king. Despite hiding among the equipment, Saul is found and presented to the people, who accept him as king with shouts of "God save the king."

Samuel explains the rights and duties of kingship, records them in a book, and stores it before the LORD. Saul returns to Gibeah, accompanied by a group of men inspired by God. However, some skeptics question his ability to lead. Soon after, Nahash the Ammonite threatens Jabeshgilead, and its inhabitants seek a humiliating treaty. The people of Jabesh request help from the Israelites, leading messengers to inform Saul. Angered by the news, the Spirit of God empowers Saul to unite the Israelites against the Ammonites, demonstrating his leadership and God's support.Saul, after being confirmed as leader by divine signs, leads Israel to victory against the Ammonites. The people, initially skeptical of Saul's leadership, now rally behind him, acknowledging his kingship with celebrations and sacrifices at Gilgal. Samuel, addressing all Israel, reflects on his own leadership and integrity, challenging anyone to accuse him of wrongdoing. The people affirm Samuel's righteousness. Samuel recounts the history of Israel's deliverance from Egypt and their cycles of sin and redemption, emphasizing the role of the LORD in their history. He warns the people about the consequences of forsaking the LORD for a human king, urging them to fear and serve the LORD faithfully to ensure their well-being and that of their king. Samuel demonstrates the LORD's power through a miraculous sign, reinforcing the gravity of their request for a king and leading the people to seek Samuel's intercession with the LORD to spare them from the consequences of their sins. Samuel reassures the people, encouraging them to remain faithful to the LORD despite their transgressions.Saul, after a year of reign, selects three thousand men for Israel's army, dividing them between himself and his son Jonathan. Jonathan attacks a Philistine outpost, prompting a Philistine response. The Israelites, outnumbered, hide in fear. Saul, waiting for Samuel, offers sacrifices prematurely, leading to Samuel's rebuke and the prophecy that Saul's kingdom will not endure. The Philistines, with a significant advantage in weaponry, prepare to battle the ill-equipped Israelites. Jonathan, without informing Saul, plans an attack on the Philistines, demonstrating the ongoing conflict and the challenges faced by Saul's leadership.Jonathan and his armor-bearer, without Saul's knowledge, plan to attack the Philistine garrison. Jonathan believes that with the LORD's help, they can overcome the Philistines regardless of their numbers. They decide to reveal themselves to the Philistines, interpreting their response as a sign from the LORD. The Philistines invite them to approach, seeing this as a divine sign, Jonathan and his armor-bearer attack, killing about twenty men. This act causes panic among the Philistines and even the earth quakes, leading to a great tumult. Saul notices the chaos and realizes Jonathan and his armor-bearer are missing. He calls for the Ark of God but then rushes to battle as the Philistine camp descends into confusion. The Israelites, including those who had previously hidden or sided with the Philistines, join Saul and Jonathan in the fight, leading to a significant victory for Israel. However, Saul's curse on anyone who eats before evening causes distress among the troops, especially when they find honey in the forest. Jonathan, unaware of his father's curse, eats some honey, which revitalizes him. He criticizes Saul's curse for hindering their victory. Despite the initial success, the Israelite forces are exhausted and, driven by hunger, they sin by eating meat with blood. Saul builds an altar to the LORD, marking his first such act.Saul, upon learning that the Israelites sinned by eating meat with blood, commands a great stone to be brought to him. He orders the people to slaughter their animals on this stone to avoid sinning against the LORD. That night, all the people complied, and Saul built an altar to the LORD, marking his first such construction. Saul then proposes a night attack on the Philistines but seeks divine guidance first. When God does not respond, Saul suspects sin within the camp and discovers Jonathan has unknowingly violated his command by eating honey. Despite Jonathan's pivotal role in the victory, Saul decrees his death, which the people vehemently oppose, saving Jonathan. Saul then ceases his pursuit of the Philistines, who return to their territory.

Saul's reign is characterized by continuous battles against surrounding enemies, securing victories and expanding Israel's territory. His family and military leaders are introduced, highlighting the structure of his reign. However, Saul's disobedience to God's commands leads to divine regret for his kingship, as revealed to Samuel. Saul's incomplete obedience in the battle against the Amalekites, sparing their king and the best livestock, directly contradicts God's command for total destruction, signaling the beginning of his downfall.to all the coasts of Israel. 19:30 And it was so, that all that saw it said, There was no such deed done nor seen from the day that the children of Israel came up out of the land of Egypt unto this day: consider of it, take advice, and speak your minds. 20:1 Then all the children of Israel went out, and the congregation was gathered together as one man, from Dan even to Beersheba, with the land of Gilead, unto the LORD in Mizpeh. 20:2 And the chief of all the people, even of all the tribes of Israel, presented themselves in the assembly of the people of God, four hundred thousand footmen that drew sword. 20:3 (Now the children of Benjamin heard that the children of Israel were gone up to Mizpeh.) Then said the children of Israel, Tell us, how was this wickedness? 20:4 And the Levite, the husband of the woman that was slain, answered and said, I came into Gibeah that belongeth to Benjamin, I and my concubine, to lodge. 20:5 And the men of Gibeah rose against me, and beset the house round about upon me by night, and thought to have slain me: and my concubine have they forced, that she is dead. 20:6 And I took my concubine, and cut her in pieces, and sent her throughout all the country of the inheritance of Israel: for they have committed lewdness and folly in Israel. 20:7 Behold, ye are all children of Israel; give here your advice and counsel. 20:8 And all the people arose as one man, saying, We will not any of us go to his tent, neither will we any of us turn into his house. 20:9 But now this shall be the thing which we will do to Gibeah; we will go up by lot against it; 20:10 And we will take ten men of an hundred throughout all the tribes of Israel, and an hundred of a thousand, and a thousand out of ten thousand, to fetch victual for the people, that they may do, when they come to Gibeah of Benjamin, according to all the folly that they have wrought in Israel. 20:11 So all the men of Israel were gathered against the city, knit together as one man. 20:12 And the tribes of Israel sent men through all the tribe of Benjamin, saying, What wickedness is this that is done among you? 20:13 Now therefore deliver us the men, the children of Belial, which are in Gibeah, that we may put them to death, and put away evil from Israel. But the children of Benjamin would not hearken to the voice of their brethren the children of Israel. 20:14 But the children of Benjamin gathered themselves together out of the cities unto Gibeah, to go out to battle against the children of Israel. 20:15 And the children of Benjamin were numbered at that time out of the cities twenty and six thousand men that drew sword, beside the inhabitants of Gibeah, which were numbered seven hundred chosen men. 20:16 Among all this people there were seven hundred chosen men lefthanded; every one could sling stones at an hair breadth, and not miss. 20:17 And the men of Israel, beside Benjamin, were numbered four hundred thousand men that drew sword: all these were men of war. 20:18 And the children of Israel arose, and went up to the house of God, and asked counsel of God, and said, Which of us shall go up first to the battle against the children of Benjamin? And the LORD said, Judah shall go up first. 20:19 And the children of Israel rose up in the morning, and encamped against Gibeah. 20:20 And the men of Israel went out to battle against Benjamin; and the men of Israel put themselves in array to fight against them at Gibeah. 20:21 And the children of Benjamin came forth out of Gibeah, and destroyed down to the ground of the Israelites that day twenty and two thousand men. 20:22 And the people the men of Israel encouraged themselves, and set their battle again in array in the place where they put themselves in array the first day. 20:23 (And the children of Israel went up and wept before the LORD until even, and asked counsel of the LORD, saying, Shall I go up again to battle against the children of Benjamin my brother? And the LORD said, Go up against him.) 20:24 And the children of Israel came near against the children of Benjamin the second day. 20:25 And Benjamin went forth against them out of Gibeah the second day, and destroyed down to the ground of the children of Israel again eighteen thousand men; all these drew the sword. 20:26 Then all the children of Israel, and all the people, went up, and came unto the house of God, and wept, and sat there before the LORD, and fasted that day until even, and offered burnt offerings and peace offerings before the LORD. 20:27 And the children of Israel enquired of the LORD, (for the ark of the covenant of God was there in those days, 20:28 And Phinehas, the son of Eleazar, the son of Aaron, stood before it in those days,) saying, Shall I yet again go out to battle against the children of Benjamin my brother, or shall I cease? And the LORD said, Go up; for to morrow I will deliver them into thine hand. 20:29 And Israel set liers in wait round about Gibeah. 20:30 And the children of Israel went up againstSamuel rose early to meet Saul, who claimed to have followed the LORD's command. However, Samuel questioned the presence of spoils from the Amalekites, which Saul admitted were spared by the people for sacrifice. Samuel rebuked Saul, emphasizing obedience over sacrifice and declaring Saul's rejection as king due to his disobedience. Saul confessed his sin, fearing the people's opinion over God's command. Despite Saul's plea for forgiveness, Samuel affirmed God's rejection of Saul as king, symbolized by the tearing of Samuel's mantle. Samuel announced that the kingdom would be given to someone better than Saul and executed Agag, the Amalekite king, before departing and mourning for Saul. God instructed Samuel to anoint a new king from Jesse's sons, leading to Samuel's journey to Bethlehem under the guise of a sacrifice. Despite initial fears, Samuel was welcomed and proceeded to evaluate Jesse's sons for kingship. God guided Samuel to look beyond appearances, emphasizing the importance of the heart in His selection.the children of Benjamin on the third day, and positioned themselves against Gibeah as before. The Benjamites attacked, killing about thirty Israelites. Believing they were winning, the Benjamites pursued the Israelites, but Israel had set an ambush around Gibeah. Israel then counterattacked, bringing ten thousand chosen men against Gibeah. The Lord helped Israel defeat Benjamin, killing 25,100 Benjamite soldiers. The Israelites had set a signal to indicate when the ambush should begin, leading to a significant defeat for the Benjamites, who were then chased and killed as they fled. In total, Benjamin lost 25,000 warriors that day. However, 600 Benjamite men escaped to the wilderness, where they remained for four months. Following this, the Israelites destroyed the cities of Benjamin, killing both people and animals and burning everything.

Israel had previously sworn not to allow their daughters to marry Benjamites, leading to a dilemma on how to provide wives for the surviving Benjamite men without breaking their oath. They lamented the near extinction of one of Israel's tribes and sought a solution that would allow the tribe of Benjamin to continue without breaking their vow. They discovered that no one from Jabeshgilead had joined the assembly, so they sent warriors to attack Jabeshgilead, killing all the males and non-virgin females, intending to find wives for the remaining Benjamites from the survivors.Samuel, following God's guidance, rejected Jesse's seven older sons for kingship, awaiting the youngest, David, who was tending sheep. Upon David's arrival, noted for his appearance and favor with God, Samuel anointed him, marking the beginning of the Spirit of the LORD's presence with David and its departure from Saul. Saul, troubled by an evil spirit, sought relief through music, leading to David's introduction to Saul's court as a skilled harpist, eventually becoming Saul's armorbearer and finding favor in his sight. David's music provided Saul temporary relief from the tormenting spirit.

The narrative shifts to the Philistine army's challenge to Israel, led by Goliath, a giant warrior, who taunted the Israelites, proposing a single combat to decide the conflict. The Israelites, including Saul, were terrified. Meanwhile, David, still a shepherd and youngest son of Jesse, was sent to the battlefront to deliver provisions to his brothers, witnessing Goliath's challenge. This sets the stage for the ensuing confrontation between David and Goliath, highlighting David's humble beginnings and his faith-driven courage that would define his legacy.Jabeshgilead provided four hundred young virgins to the Israelites as wives for the Benjamite survivors. These women, who had never been with a man, were brought to Shiloh in Canaan. The Israelites then reached out to the Benjamites in Rimmon, offering peace and the women of Jabeshgilead as wives. Despite this, there were not enough women for every Benjamite, leading the elders to seek additional solutions to prevent the extinction of the tribe of Benjamin. They noted a yearly feast of the LORD in Shiloh, suggesting the Benjamites could find wives among the daughters of Shiloh during the festivities. This plan was executed, allowing the Benjamites to rebuild their tribe and cities. The narrative concludes with a statement about the period's lawlessness, indicating a time when Israel had no king, and everyone did as they saw fit.

The Book of Ruth begins during a famine, prompting Elimelech and his family to move from Bethlehem to Moab. After Elimelech's death, Naomi, his widow, decides to return to Bethlehem with her daughters-in-law, Orpah and Ruth. Despite Naomi's urging, Ruth chooses to stay with Naomi, adopting her people and God as her own. This marks the beginning of Ruth's journey with Naomi back to Bethlehem, highlighting themes of loyalty, family, and faith.David rose early, left the sheep with a keeper, and went as Jesse commanded. He arrived at the battlefront as the armies prepared to fight. David left his belongings with the keeper and greeted his brothers. While talking to them, Goliath, the Philistine champion, issued his challenge, which David heard. The Israelites were terrified of Goliath, but David was curious about the reward for defeating him, questioning why the Philistine should defy God's armies. Eliab, David's eldest brother, scolded him for coming, but David insisted on understanding the situation.

David's words reached Saul, who summoned him. David offered to fight Goliath, despite Saul's doubts due to David's youth. David recounted his experiences of killing a lion and a bear while shepherding, asserting that God who delivered him from those beasts would also deliver him from Goliath. Saul consented, attempting to equip David with armor, which David declined, unaccustomed to it. Instead, David chose five smooth stones and approached Goliath with his sling.

Goliath mocked David, but David responded confidently, declaring that he came in the name of the Lord and that the battle belonged to God. He predicted Goliath's defeat and the Philistines' rout to demonstrate God's power. As Goliath advanced, David quickly slung a stone, striking Goliath and proving God's might.David prevailed over the Philistine with a sling and a stone, striking him in the forehead, causing him to fall face down to the ground. Without a sword, David ran to the Philistine, took his sword, killed him, and cut off his head. The Philistines fled upon seeing their champion defeated. The Israelites pursued them to the gates of Ekron, with many Philistines falling along the way. David brought the Philistine's head to Jerusalem and stored his armor in his tent.

Saul, intrigued by David's bravery, inquired about his lineage. David introduced himself as the son of Jesse from Bethlehem. Following this event, Jonathan, Saul's son, formed a deep bond with David, signifying their friendship by exchanging garments and weapons. David's success in Saul's missions earned him a high regard among the people and Saul's servants.

However, Saul became envious of David when the women celebrated David's victories in song, attributing more victories to David than to Saul. This envy led Saul to fear and resent David, even attempting to harm him. Despite Saul's hostility, David continued to serve wisely, gaining the love of the people and further inciting Saul's fear.

Saul offered his daughter Merab to David in marriage, hoping to entangle him with the Philistines, but later gave Merab to another. Michal, Saul's other daughter, loved David, and Saul saw an opportunity to use this to his advantage, again hoping to bring about David's downfall through marriage. Despite the king's plots, David remained humble and cautious, questioning his worthiness of such honor. Saul's servants relayed David's concerns, but Saul persisted in his attempts to ensnare David through the marriage to Michal.Saul told his servants to convey to David that he did not desire any dowry for his daughter's hand in marriage, but rather a hundred Philistine foreskins, aiming to endanger David. David, pleased to become the king's son-in-law before the deadline, killed two hundred Philistines and presented their foreskins to Saul, securing Michal as his wife. Saul recognized the LORD's favor on David and Michal's love for him, which intensified his fear and enmity towards David. Despite this, David continued to excel, earning high regard from all.

Saul commanded Jonathan and his servants to kill David. However, Jonathan, fond of David, warned him of Saul's intentions, advising him to hide. Jonathan then defended David to Saul, highlighting his loyalty and the injustice of killing him without cause. Saul, persuaded by Jonathan, vowed not to kill David, who returned to Saul's service. Yet, the conflict resumed, and David achieved victory over the Philistines again. An evil spirit from the LORD prompted Saul to attempt to kill David with a javelin, but David escaped.

Michal helped David flee from Saul's attempt on his life, deceiving her father by claiming David was sick and using an image to simulate David in bed. When Saul discovered the deception, Michal lied to protect David, who then sought refuge with Samuel in Ramah. Saul's messengers, sent to capture David, were overcome by the prophetic spirit when they encountered Samuel and the prophets. Saul himself experienced the same when he pursued David, leading to his prophetic behavior in Naioth, which surprised many.

David, still in danger, sought Jonathan's help, questioning what he had done to earn Saul's wrath.Jonathan assured David that he would not die, as his father, Saul, would have informed him of any plans against David. David, however, felt his life was in imminent danger. They devised a plan for David to hide during the new moon feast. If Saul reacted angrily to David's absence, it would confirm his malicious intentions. Jonathan agreed to secretly inform David of Saul's disposition, using a coded message involving arrows. If the arrows were shot a certain way, it would signal David to flee, as Saul intended harm. Jonathan and David reaffirmed their covenant, emphasizing their loyalty and friendship. During the feast, Saul noticed David's absence and questioned Jonathan, who lied, saying David had gone to Bethlehem for a family sacrifice. Enraged, Saul insulted Jonathan and ordered him to bring David to be killed. Jonathan defended David, causing Saul to become further enraged.was steadfastly minded to go with her, then she left speaking unto her. So they two went until they came to Bethlehem. The next day, Jonathan and David executed their plan to communicate through a coded message involving arrows. Jonathan's actions confirmed to David that Saul intended to kill him. They shared a heartfelt farewell, reaffirming their covenant before David fled.

David then went to Nob, where he met Ahimelech the priest. Fearing for his safety and on a secret mission from the king, David requested food and a weapon. Ahimelech provided him with the holy bread and Goliath's sword. David's presence in Nob was noted by Doeg the Edomite, a servant of Saul, which later had significant consequences.

Fearing Saul, David fled to Achish, king of Gath, but fearing recognition, he feigned madness to escape danger. David then sought refuge in the cave of Adullam, attracting followers in distress, forming a group of about four hundred men. David then sought refuge in the cave of Adullam, attracting followers in distress, forming a group of about four hundred men. Seeking safety for his family, David arranged for them to stay with the king of Moab. During this time, the prophet Gad advised David to leave the stronghold and go into the land of Judah. David departed, coming into the forest of Hareth.

When Saul heard of David's whereabouts, he accused his servants of conspiring against him with David. Doeg the Edomite reported seeing David at Nob, where Ahimelech had inquired of the LORD for him, provided him with food, and given him Goliath's sword. Saul summoned Ahimelech and all the priests from Nob, accusing them of conspiring with David. Despite Ahimelech's defense, Saul ordered their death. Doeg executed the king's command, killing eighty-five priests and attacking the city of Nob, killing men, women, children, and livestock. Only Abiathar, a son of Ahimelech, escaped and informed David of the massacre.

David, realizing his presence had endangered the priests, offered Abiathar protection. They then learned of the Philistines attacking Keilah and robbing the threshing floors. David inquired of the LORD, who instructed him to attack the Philistines and save Keilah. Despite his men's fear, David led them to victory, saving Keilah. Saul, hearing of David's presence in Keilah, planned to besiege the city. David, aware of Saul's intentions, inquired of the LORD, who confirmed that the people of Keilah would betray him. David and his men then left Keilah, continuing to evade Saul.David and his men, now six hundred in number, left Keilah and wandered, evading Saul. Despite Saul's relentless pursuit, God protected David. In the wilderness of Ziph, Jonathan met David, encouraging him and reaffirming their covenant, with Jonathan acknowledging David's future as king. The Ziphites betrayed David's location to Saul, but David managed to escape to the wilderness of Maon. Saul nearly captured David there, but had to divert his attention to a Philistine invasion, allowing David to move to Engedi.

When Saul resumed his pursuit, he unknowingly entered the cave where David and his men were hiding. David had the opportunity to kill Saul but chose instead to cut off a piece of Saul's robe, later expressing regret even for this act, demonstrating his respect for Saul as the Lord's anointed. David confronted Saul, showing the piece of the robe as proof of his loyalty. Saul acknowledged David's righteousness, and David swore not to harm Saul's descendants or wipe out his name.
