And he said unto the kinsman, Naomi, that is come again out of the country of Moab, selleth a parcel of land, which was our brother Elimelech's: And I thought to advertise thee, saying, Buy it before the inhabitants, and before the elders of my people. If thou wilt redeem it, redeem it: but if thou wilt not redeem it, then tell me, that I may know: for there is none to redeem it beside thee; and I am after thee. And he said, I will redeem it. Then said Boaz, What day thou buyest the field of the hand of Naomi, thou must buy it also of Ruth the Moabitess, the wife of the dead, to raise up the name of the dead upon his inheritance. And the kinsman said, I cannot redeem it for myself, lest I mar mine own inheritance: redeem thou my right to thyself; for I cannot redeem it. Now this was the manner in former time in Israel concerning redeeming and concerning changing, for to confirm all things; a man plucked off his shoe, and gave it to his neighbour: and this was a testimony in Israel. Therefore the kinsman said unto Boaz, Buy it for thee. So he drew off his shoe. And Boaz said unto the elders, and unto all the people, Ye are witnesses this day, that I have bought all that was Elimelech's, and all that was Chilion's and Mahlon's, of the hand of Naomi. Moreover Ruth the Moabitess, the wife of Mahlon, have I purchased to be my wife, to raise up the name of the dead upon his inheritance, that the name of the dead be not cut off from among his brethren, and from the gate of his place: ye are witnesses this day. And all the people that were in the gate, and the elders, said, We are witnesses. The LORD make the woman that is come into thine house like Rachel and like Leah, which two did build the house of Israel: and do thou worthily in Ephratah, and be famous in Bethlehem: And let thy house be like the house of Pharez, whom Tamar bare unto Judah, of the seed which the LORD shall give thee of this young woman. So Boaz took Ruth, and she was his wife: and when he went in unto her, the LORD gave her conception, and she bare a son. And the women said unto Naomi, Blessed be the LORD, which hath not left thee this day without a kinsman, that his name may be famous in Israel."After Saul's acknowledgment of David's righteousness and their reconciliation, David remained cautious. Saul returned home, while David and his men moved to a stronghold. The narrative then shifts to the death of Samuel, a significant event mourned by all Israelites, leading David to the wilderness of Paran.

In Paran, David encounters Nabal, a wealthy but churlish man, during sheep shearing in Carmel. David, seeking provisions for his men, sends messengers to Nabal with a polite request for assistance, highlighting the peaceful coexistence between his men and Nabal's shepherds. Nabal, however, dismisses David's messengers rudely, questioning David's legitimacy and refusing any aid. This response prompts David to prepare for retaliation against Nabal.

One of Nabal's servants informs Abigail, Nabal's wise and beautiful wife, of the situation, emphasizing the good treatment they received from David's men and the impending danger due to Nabal's actions. Abigail quickly acts to avert the crisis, preparing a generous offering for David and setting out to meet him without informing Nabal.

As Abigail meets David, she presents her offering and pleads for forgiveness, highlighting the folly of her husband and the potential for unnecessary bloodshed. David, moved by Abigail's wisdom and the provisions, halts his plans for retaliation, acknowledging the intervention as preventing him from committing unnecessary violence. This encounter not only averts a conflict but also showcases the themes of wisdom, intervention, and the consequences of actions within the narrative.Abigail prostrated herself before David, pleading for forgiveness and urging him not to regard her husband Nabal's foolishness. She highlighted that the Lord had prevented David from committing bloodshed and avenging himself. Abigail's wise intervention led David to accept her offering and forgive her, acknowledging that her actions had spared him from unnecessary violence. David praised the Lord for sending Abigail to him and recognized the value of her advice.

After their encounter, Abigail returned to Nabal, who was unaware of the near disaster due to his drunkenness. The next morning, when sober, Nabal learned of the events from Abigail and was struck with fear, leading to his death ten days later. David, upon hearing of Nabal's demise, praised the Lord for avenging the insult and protecting him from wrongdoing. Subsequently, David proposed to Abigail, who humbly accepted, becoming his wife along with Ahinoam of Jezreel. Meanwhile, Saul had given Michal, David's wife, to another man.

The narrative then shifts to Saul's continued pursuit of David, with the Ziphites informing Saul of David's location. Saul, with a large force, sought David in the wilderness. David, aware of Saul's approach, sent out spies and then, with Abishai, approached Saul's camp at night, finding Saul and his men asleep.Abishai and David found Saul asleep in his camp, with his spear by his side. Abishai offered to kill Saul with the spear, but David refused, citing the sin of raising a hand against the Lord's anointed. Instead, David took Saul's spear and water jug as proof of their presence. They left without waking anyone, as the Lord had caused a deep sleep to fall upon the camp. From a safe distance, David called out to Saul's commander, Abner, criticizing him for not protecting Saul. Saul recognized David's voice and admitted his wrongdoing, promising not to harm David. David then returned Saul's spear, emphasizing his innocence and asking for the Lord's judgment on their actions. Saul blessed David, acknowledging his future success, and they parted ways. David, fearing for his life, decided to seek refuge in Philistine territory with his followers and their families. He settled in Ziklag, given to him by Achish, king of Gath. David lived there for over a year, conducting raids against neighboring tribes but deceiving Achish about his activities.David misled Achish by claiming his raids were against Judah, ensuring Achish believed he had made himself detestable to his own people, securing his place as Achish's servant indefinitely. As the Philistines prepared for war against Israel, Achish enlisted David and his men, promising David a permanent position. Meanwhile, Saul, having expelled mediums and wizards from Israel and finding himself without guidance from the Lord, sought a medium in Endor to summon Samuel. Despite his disguise, the medium recognized Saul, who reassured her of her safety. She summoned Samuel, who rebuked Saul for disturbing him and predicted Saul's defeat and death in the upcoming battle due to his disobedience to God. Saul, weakened and terrified by the prophecy, was urged to eat by the medium and his servants, regaining his strength before leaving. The narrative highlights the contrasting fortunes of David and Saul, with David gaining favor through deception and Saul facing despair and defeat for his disobedience.As the Philistine armies gathered at Aphek, the Israelites camped near a fountain in Jezreel. The Philistine lords, marching by hundreds and thousands, questioned the presence of David and his men, who were with Achish at the rear. Achish defended David, claiming he had found no fault in him since his defection. However, the Philistine princes insisted David be sent back, fearing he might turn against them in battle to regain Saul's favor, especially since David was celebrated in Israel for his military exploits.

Achish, reluctantly agreeing with his princes, asked David to leave the battlefield to avoid displeasing them. David, puzzled, questioned what wrong he had done to be denied the chance to fight Israel's enemies. Achish, acknowledging David's loyalty, still ordered him to leave with his men at dawn. Thus, David and his men departed back to Philistine territory, while the Philistines moved towards Jezreel.

Upon returning to Ziklag, David found it devastated by the Amalekites, who had taken their families captive without killing anyone. Overwhelmed with grief, David and his men wept. Facing mutiny from his own men, David sought strength in God. Consulting with the priest Abiathar, David was assured by God of victory in pursuing the Amalekites.

David, with 600 men, pursued but only 400 could continue after a strenuous march. They found an Egyptian slave, abandoned by the Amalekites, who, after being revived with food and water, agreed to lead David to the Amalekite camp. There, David found the Amalekites celebrating their plunder from Philistine and Judah territories. David attacked, defeating them and recovering all that was taken, including his two wives.4:15 "And he shall be unto thee a restorer of thy life, and a nourisher of thine old age: for thy daughter in law, which loveth thee, which is better to thee than seven sons, hath born him. 4:16 And Naomi took the child, and laid it in her bosom, and became nurse unto it. 4:17 And the women her neighbours gave it a name, saying, There is a son born to Naomi; and they called his name Obed: he is the father of Jesse, the father of David. 4:18 Now these are the generations of Pharez: Pharez begat Hezron, 4:19 And Hezron begat Ram, and Ram begat Amminadab, 4:20 And Amminadab begat Nahshon, and Nahshon begat Salmon, 4:21 And Salmon begat Boaz, and Boaz begat Obed, 4:22 And Obed begat Jesse, and Jesse begat David. The First Book of Samuel Otherwise Called: The First Book of the Kings 1:1 Now there was a certain man of Ramathaimzophim, of mount Ephraim, and his name was Elkanah, the son of Jeroham, the son of Elihu, the son of Tohu, the son of Zuph, an Ephrathite: 1:2 And he had two wives; the name of the one was Hannah, and the name of the other Peninnah: and Peninnah had children, but Hannah had no children. 1:3 And this man went up out of his city yearly to worship and to sacrifice unto the LORD of hosts in Shiloh. And the two sons of Eli, Hophni and Phinehas, the priests of the LORD, were there. 1:4 And when the time was that Elkanah offered, he gave to Peninnah his wife, and to all her sons and her daughters, portions: 1:5 But unto Hannah he gave a worthy portion; for he loved Hannah: but the LORD had shut up her womb. 1:6 And her adversary also provoked her sore, for to make her fret, because the LORD had shut up her womb. 1:7 And as he did so year by year, when she went up to the house of the LORD, so she provoked her; therefore she wept, and did not eat. 1:8 Then said Elkanah her husband to her, Hannah, why weepest thou? and why eatest thou not? and why is thy heart grieved? am not I better to thee than ten sons? 1:9 So Hannah rose up after they had eaten in Shiloh, and after they had drunk. Now Eli the priest sat upon a seat by a post of the temple of the LORD. 1:10 And she was in bitterness of soul, and prayed unto the LORD, and wept sore. 1:11 And she vowed a vow, and said, O LORD of hosts, if thou wilt indeed look on the affliction of thine handmaid, and remember me, and not forget thine handmaid, but wilt give unto thine handmaid a man child, then I will give him unto the LORD all the days of his life, and there shall no razor come upon his head. 1:12 And it came to pass, as she continued praying before the LORD, that Eli marked her mouth. 1:13 Now Hannah, she spake in her heart; only her lips moved, but her voice was not heard: therefore Eli thought she had been drunken. 1:14 And Eli said unto her, How long wilt thou be drunken? put away thy wine from thee. 1:15 And Hannah answered and said, No, my lord, I am a woman of a sorrowful spirit: I have drunk neither wine nor strong drink, but have poured out my soul before the LORD. 1:16 Count not thine handmaid for a daughter of Belial: for out of the abundance of my complaint and grief have I spoken hitherto. 1:17 Then Eli answered and said, Go in peace: and the God of Israel grant thee thy petition that thou hast asked of him. 1:18 And she said, Let thine handmaid find grace in thy sight. So the woman went her way, and did eat, and her countenance was no more sad. 1:19 And they rose up in the morning early, and worshipped before the LORD, and returned, and came to their house to Ramah: and Elkanah knew Hannah his wife; and the LORD remembered her. 1:20 Wherefore it came to pass, when the time was come about after Hannah had conceived, that she bare a son, and called his name Samuel, saying, Because I have asked him of the LORD. 1:21 And the man Elkanah, and all his house, went up to offer unto the LORD the yearly sacrifice, and his vow. 1:22 But Hannah went not up; for she said unto her husband, I will not go up until the child be weaned, and then I will bring him, that he may appear before the LORD, and there abide for ever. 1:23 And Elkanah her husband said unto her, Do what seemeth thee good; tarry until thou have weaned him; only the LORD establish his word. So the woman abode, and gave her son suck until she weaned him. 1:24 And when she had weaned him, she took him up with her, with three bullocks, and one ephah of flour, and a bottle of wine, and brought him unto the house of the LORD in Shiloh: and the child was young. 1:25 And they slew a bullock, and brought the child to Eli. 1:26 And she said, Oh my lord, as thy soul liveth, my lord, I am the woman that stood by thee here, praying unto the LORD. 1:27 For this child I prayed; and the LORD hath given me my petition which I asked of him: 1:28 Therefore also I have lent him to the LORD; as long as he liveth he shall be lent to the LORD. And he worshipped the LORD there. 2:1 And Hannah prayed, and said, My heart rejoiceth in the LORD, mine horn is exalted in the LORD: my mouth is enlarged overDavid recovered everything the Amalekites had taken, including his two wives. Nothing was missing, whether small or great, sons or daughters, spoil, or anything else they had taken. David also took all the flocks and herds, declaring them as his spoil. When he returned to the 200 men too exhausted to follow, he greeted them. However, some men who went with David argued that those who stayed behind should not share in the spoil, except for their wives and children. David countered, saying it was wrong to withhold the LORD's gifts, emphasizing that everyone should share alike, regardless of their role. This principle became a statute in Israel.

David then sent part of the spoil to the elders of Judah as a gift, acknowledging their support. The narrative shifts to the battle where the Philistines fought against Israel, leading to the death of Saul and his sons on Mount Gilboa. Saul, wounded by archers, killed himself to avoid capture. His armor-bearer and all his men died with him. The Philistines occupied the abandoned Israelite cities. They found Saul and his sons' bodies, beheaded Saul, and sent his armor to the temple of Ashtaroth, pinning his body to the wall of Bethshan. The men of Jabeshgilead retrieved and buried them, mourning for seven days. The account transitions to the Second Book of Samuel, starting with David learning about Saul's death after returning from defeating the Amalekites.From the battle, many people also fell and died; Saul and Jonathan, his son, are dead as well. David inquired of the young man how he knew Saul and Jonathan were dead. The young man explained that he found Saul leaning on his spear at Mount Gilboa, with chariots and horsemen close behind him. Saul, seeing him, asked for death to escape his anguish, as he was still alive. The young man obliged, killing Saul and taking his crown and bracelet to David. Upon hearing this, David and his men mourned, wept, and fasted for Saul, Jonathan, and the fallen of the LORD and Israel. David questioned the young man's origins, learning he was an Amalekite, and then questioned his actions against the LORD's anointed. David ordered the young man's death for his actions. David lamented over Saul and Jonathan, urging the children of Judah to learn the bow, as written in the book of Jasher. He mourned the mighty fallen in battle, praised Saul and Jonathan's virtues, and lamented the loss of war's instruments.

David sought the LORD's guidance on whether to go up to the cities of Judah, receiving affirmation to go to Hebron. There, David and his men settled, and David was anointed king over Judah. David thanked the men of Jabeshgilead for burying Saul, offering kindness in return. He encouraged them to be strong as Saul was dead, and he was anointed king over Judah. Meanwhile, Abner, Saul's army captain, made Ishbosheth, Saul's son, king over Israel, excluding Judah. Ishbosheth reigned for two years, while David reigned in Hebron over Judah for seven years and six months. Conflict arose between Abner's forces and those loyal to David, leading to a confrontation at Gibeon.At the pool of Gibeon, forces led by Abner and Joab met. Abner suggested a contest between young warriors from each side, leading to a tragic outcome where all participants killed each other, marking the place as Helkathhazzurim. A fierce battle ensued, with Abner's forces being defeated by David's. Asahel, swift and determined, pursued Abner but was killed when Abner, reluctant, struck him. This halted Asahel's pursuit, and his death caused a momentary pause in the battle. Joab and Abishai continued the chase until Abner called for a cessation, questioning the endless conflict. Joab agreed, signaling a temporary peace. Abner and his men retreated, while Joab accounted for his losses, including Asahel, who was buried in Bethlehem. The conflict between the houses of Saul and David persisted, with David's position strengthening over time.

David's family grew in Hebron, marking the births of his sons. Meanwhile, tensions within Saul's house escalated when Ishbosheth accused Abner of disloyalty, leading to Abner's angry declaration of shifting his support to David, aiming to unite Israel under David's rule. Abner's negotiations with David began, setting the stage for a significant alliance, contingent upon the return of Michal, Saul's daughter and David's former wife.David demanded the return of his wife Michal from Ishbosheth, Saul's son, as a condition to meet. Ishbosheth complied, sending Michal back to David, despite her husband Phaltiel's distress. Abner, having fallen out with Ishbosheth, began negotiations to unite Israel under David's rule, citing divine support for David. He successfully rallied the elders of Israel and the tribe of Benjamin to David's cause. Abner's visit to David in Hebron, accompanied by twenty men, ended with a feast and a promise to gather all Israel in allegiance to David. However, upon learning of Abner's visit and subsequent departure in peace, Joab, David's military commander, questioned David's decision, suspecting Abner's motives. Joab, acting without David's knowledge, lured Abner back and killed him in revenge for his brother Asahel's death. David distanced himself and his kingdom from Abner's murder, placing the blame on Joab and mourning Abner's death publicly, declaring his innocence before God. The people's reaction to David's mourning and his refusal to eat until sunset was favorable, reinforcing his leadership. David lamented the loss of Abner, recognizing him as a great leader in Israel, and expressed his difficulty in controlling Joab and his brother. Following Abner's death, Saul's son and the Israelites were disheartened. Meanwhile, Saul's grandson Mephibosheth became lame during a hasty escape after hearing of Saul and Jonathan's deaths. Two captains, Rechab and Baanah, saw an opportunity amidst the turmoil."mine enemies; because I rejoice in thy salvation. And Elkanah went to Ramah to his house. And the child did minister unto the LORD before Eli the priest. Now the sons of Eli were sons of Belial; they knew not the LORD. And the priests' custom with the people was, that, when any man offered sacrifice, the priest's servant came, while the flesh was in seething, with a fleshhook of three teeth in his hand; and he struck it into the pan, or kettle, or caldron, or pot; all that the fleshhook brought up the priest took for himself. So they did in Shiloh unto all the Israelites that came thither. Also before they burnt the fat, the priest's servant came, and said to the man that sacrificed, 'Give flesh to roast for the priest; for he will not have sodden flesh of thee, but raw.' And if any man said unto him, 'Let them not fail to burn the fat presently, and then take as much as thy soul desireth;' then he would answer him, 'Nay; but thou shalt give it me now: and if not, I will take it by force.' Wherefore the sin of the young men was very great before the LORD: for men abhorred the offering of the LORD. But Samuel ministered before the LORD, being a child, girded with a linen ephod. Moreover his mother made him a little coat, and brought it to him from year to year, when she came up with her husband to offer the yearly sacrifice. And Eli blessed Elkanah and his wife, and said, 'The LORD give thee seed of this woman for the loan which is lent to the LORD.' And they went unto their own home. And the LORD visited Hannah, so that she conceived, and bare three sons and two daughters. And the child Samuel grew before the LORD. Now Eli was very old, and heard all that his sons did unto all Israel; and how they lay with the women that assembled at the door of the tabernacle of the congregation. And he said unto them, 'Why do ye such things? for I hear of your evil dealings by all this people. Nay, my sons; for it is no good report that I hear: ye make the LORD's people to transgress. If one man sin against another, the judge shall judge him: but if a man sin against the LORD, who shall intreat for him?' Notwithstanding they hearkened not unto the voice of their father, because the LORD would slay them. And the child Samuel grew on, and was in favour both with the LORD, and also with men. And there came a man of God unto Eli, and said unto him, 'Thus saith the LORD, Did I plainly appear unto the house of thy father, when they were in Egypt in Pharaoh's house? And did I choose him out of all the tribes of Israel to be my priest, to offer upon mine altar, to burn incense, to wear an ephod before me? and did I give unto the house of thy father all the offerings made by fire of the children of Israel? Wherefore kick ye at my sacrifice and at mine offering, which I have commanded in my habitation; and honourest thy sons above me, to make yourselves fat with the chiefest of all the offerings of Israel my people? Wherefore the LORD God of Israel saith, I said indeed that thy house, and the house of thy father, should walk before me for ever: but now the LORD saith, Be it far from me; for them that honour me I will honour, and they that despise me shall be lightly esteemed. Behold, the days come, that I will cut off thine arm, and the arm of thy father's house, that there shall not be an old man in thine house. And thou shalt see an enemy in my habitation, in all the wealth which God shall give Israel: and there shall not be an old man in thine house for ever. And the man of thine, whom I shall not cut off from mine altar, shall be to consume thine eyes, and to grieve thine h"Rechab and Baanah entered Ishbosheth's house under the guise of fetching wheat, but instead, they assassinated him in his bed and beheaded him. They then brought Ishbosheth's head to David in Hebron, claiming they had avenged David against Saul's lineage. David, however, condemned their actions, stating that he had previously executed someone for claiming to kill Saul, emphasizing that he would not reward murder. David ordered the execution of Rechab and Baanah, their bodies displayed in Hebron, but Ishbosheth's head was respectfully buried in Abner's tomb.

Subsequently, all Israelite tribes united under David's leadership in Hebron, acknowledging him as their kin and recalling his past leadership. They anointed David as king, marking the beginning of his 40-year reign, first in Hebron over Judah and then in Jerusalem over all Israel and Judah. David captured Jerusalem from the Jebusites, establishing it as his capital, and expanded the city. Recognizing David's leadership, Hiram, king of Tyre, sent materials and craftsmen to build David a palace, signifying God's blessing on David's kingship. David's family also grew, with several children born to him in Jerusalem.

When the Philistines learned of David's anointment, they sought to challenge him, but David, seeking God's guidance, defeated them in multiple encounters, demonstrating his reliance on divine support for victory.
